<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da"><span data-offset-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da:0">Dictionaries are also often called </span><span data-offset-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da:1">**maps**</span><span data-offset-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da:2">, </span><span data-offset-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da:3">**hashmaps**</span><span data-offset-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da:4">, </span><span data-offset-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da:5">**lookup tables**</span><span data-offset-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da:6">, or </span><span data-offset-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da:7">**associative arrays**</span><span data-offset-key="10244a1b74a645558077fc64067702da:8">. They allow for the efficient lookup, insertion, and deletion of any object associated with a given key.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="7b67d1f281e4478b8d091a790a5bc1e4"><span data-offset-key="7b67d1f281e4478b8d091a790a5bc1e4:0">Phone books make a decent real-world analog for dictionary objects. They allow you to quickly retrieve the information (phone number) associated with a given key (a person’s name). Instead of having to read a phone book front to back to find someone’s number, you can jump more or less directly to a name and look up the associated information.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="c09c184875dc46e894a70d72aa3d6a7a"><span data-offset-key="c09c184875dc46e894a70d72aa3d6a7a:0">This analogy breaks down somewhat when it comes to </span><span data-offset-key="c09c184875dc46e894a70d72aa3d6a7a:1">_how_</span><span data-offset-key="c09c184875dc46e894a70d72aa3d6a7a:2"> the information is organized to allow for fast lookups. But the fundamental performance characteristics hold. Dictionaries allow you to quickly find the information associated with a given key.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="47cad55fa3c348a09cda18cca2211f4f"><span data-offset-key="47cad55fa3c348a09cda18cca2211f4f:0">Dictionaries are one of the most important and frequently used data structures in computer science. So, how does Python handle dictionaries? Let’s take a tour of the dictionary implementations available in core Python and the Python standard library.</span></span></span>

    ### `dict`: Your Go-To Dictionary[](https://realpython.com/python-data-structures/#dict-your-go-to-dictionary "Permanent link")​Because dictionaries are so important, Python features a robust dictionary implementation that’s built directly into the core language: the [`dict`](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#mapping-types-dict) data type.​Python also provides some useful **syntactic sugar** for working with dictionaries in your programs. For example, the curly-brace ({ }) dictionary expression syntax and [dictionary comprehensions](https://realpython.com/iterate-through-dictionary-python/#using-comprehensions) allow you to conveniently define new dictionary objects:​​​​```py phonebook = {     "bob": 7387,     "alice": 3719,     "jack": 7052, }​​ squares = {x: x * x for x in range(6)}​​ phonebook["alice"]3719​​ squares{0: 0, 1: 1, 2: 4, 3: 9, 4: 16, 5: 25}` ​​

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="edc7b67e9fb84356a75ed749c5d8470a"><span data-offset-key="edc7b67e9fb84356a75ed749c5d8470a:0">`​`</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e26d0f4b2cb8481bb0f4ff476791f471"><span data-offset-key="e26d0f4b2cb8481bb0f4ff476791f471:0">There are some restrictions on which objects can be used as valid keys.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="d601fddb6df44fa38e1bc71394ff95ad"><span data-offset-key="d601fddb6df44fa38e1bc71394ff95ad:0">Python’s dictionaries are indexed by keys that can be of any </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/glossary.html#term-hashable" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="b3451cffc34c44ff8dfce6ef84008479"><span data-offset-key="b3451cffc34c44ff8dfce6ef84008479:0">hashable</span></span></a><span data-key="d5fcaa57f5b44c239165ca8a82c14ea2"><span data-offset-key="d5fcaa57f5b44c239165ca8a82c14ea2:0"> type. A </span><span data-offset-key="d5fcaa57f5b44c239165ca8a82c14ea2:1">**hashable**</span><span data-offset-key="d5fcaa57f5b44c239165ca8a82c14ea2:2"> object has a hash value that never changes during its lifetime (see </span><span data-offset-key="d5fcaa57f5b44c239165ca8a82c14ea2:3">`__hash__`</span><span data-offset-key="d5fcaa57f5b44c239165ca8a82c14ea2:4">), and it can be compared to other objects (see </span><span data-offset-key="d5fcaa57f5b44c239165ca8a82c14ea2:5">`__eq__`</span><span data-offset-key="d5fcaa57f5b44c239165ca8a82c14ea2:6">). Hashable objects that compare as equal must have the same hash value.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="92686a62943b4c9ba821a3f221f9dc87"><span data-offset-key="92686a62943b4c9ba821a3f221f9dc87:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/courses/immutability-python/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="90d912fff83545d38d6bd957141c5164"><span data-offset-key="90d912fff83545d38d6bd957141c5164:0"><strong>Immutable</strong></span><span data-offset-key="90d912fff83545d38d6bd957141c5164:1"> types</span></span></a><span data-key="2abf976144d94389bde7b55c4e904860"><span data-offset-key="2abf976144d94389bde7b55c4e904860:0"> like </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-strings/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="28691198bfd04af694bfcc4fa3f2f971"><span data-offset-key="28691198bfd04af694bfcc4fa3f2f971:0">strings</span></span></a><span data-key="cf5b0395d67047d397b4ee6e98bb567c"><span data-offset-key="cf5b0395d67047d397b4ee6e98bb567c:0"> and </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-data-types/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="80b3a2a454cf4a26876413f6b8017658"><span data-offset-key="80b3a2a454cf4a26876413f6b8017658:0">numbers</span></span></a><span data-key="ebd891832b7249b083174baeddb6ecd1"><span data-offset-key="ebd891832b7249b083174baeddb6ecd1:0"> are hashable and work well as dictionary keys. You can also use </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-lists-tuples/#python-tuples" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="6db169382a4a4cd188f71cf10759e9a4"><span data-offset-key="6db169382a4a4cd188f71cf10759e9a4:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">tuple</code></span><span data-offset-key="6db169382a4a4cd188f71cf10759e9a4:1"> objects</span></span></a><span data-key="fd0372f38c1b4c5281962be81cdebe40"><span data-offset-key="fd0372f38c1b4c5281962be81cdebe40:0"> as dictionary keys as long as they contain only hashable types themselves.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="2fde59e4b3b54be7bbea9034eb3cfa2a"><span data-offset-key="2fde59e4b3b54be7bbea9034eb3cfa2a:0">For most use cases, Python’s built-in dictionary implementation will do everything you need. Dictionaries are highly optimized and underlie many parts of the language. For example, </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-scope-legb-rule/#class-and-instance-attributes-scope" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="868be99a3df74a7c89a46dd64b2d697d"><span data-offset-key="868be99a3df74a7c89a46dd64b2d697d:0">class attributes</span></span></a><span data-key="a91a862cccd7400abf45d983c7817c8d"><span data-offset-key="a91a862cccd7400abf45d983c7817c8d:0"> and variables in a </span></span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Call_stack#Structure" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="ba7b14743de949b19545af11480c1f99"><span data-offset-key="ba7b14743de949b19545af11480c1f99:0">stack frame</span></span></a><span data-key="3efacef173d441b4bcba5d92daf36545"><span data-offset-key="3efacef173d441b4bcba5d92daf36545:0"> are both stored internally in dictionaries.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="22a83724922940e6a84546daba62db2f"><span data-offset-key="22a83724922940e6a84546daba62db2f:0">Python dictionaries are based on a well-tested and finely tuned hash table implementation that provides the performance characteristics you’d expect: </span><span data-offset-key="22a83724922940e6a84546daba62db2f:1">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="22a83724922940e6a84546daba62db2f:2">(1) time complexity for lookup, insert, update, and delete operations in the average case.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="1805a9d0b93641d4b49a9045bafb771c"><span data-offset-key="1805a9d0b93641d4b49a9045bafb771c:0">There’s little reason not to use the standard </span><span data-offset-key="1805a9d0b93641d4b49a9045bafb771c:1">`dict`</span><span data-offset-key="1805a9d0b93641d4b49a9045bafb771c:2"> implementation included with Python. However, specialized third-party dictionary implementations exist, such as </span></span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skip_list" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="bd3987922de04f09b9377d280ba0cb3c"><span data-offset-key="bd3987922de04f09b9377d280ba0cb3c:0">skip lists</span></span></a><span data-key="efad6a7ab22946588632eefe5681ba97"><span data-offset-key="efad6a7ab22946588632eefe5681ba97:0"> or </span></span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B-tree" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="6d1043e3fc8546b0973354d923d41a81"><span data-offset-key="6d1043e3fc8546b0973354d923d41a81:0">B-tree–based</span></span></a><span data-key="a1cbfe46965f4b36bfc6292a726c6722"><span data-offset-key="a1cbfe46965f4b36bfc6292a726c6722:0"> dictionaries.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="67fd6a47565041a7b18f6d5224a13667"><span data-offset-key="67fd6a47565041a7b18f6d5224a13667:0">Besides plain </span><span data-offset-key="67fd6a47565041a7b18f6d5224a13667:1">`dict`</span><span data-offset-key="67fd6a47565041a7b18f6d5224a13667:2"> objects, Python’s standard library also includes a number of specialized dictionary implementations. These specialized dictionaries are all based on the built-in dictionary class (and share its performance characteristics) but also include some additional convenience features.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="a777183a3d7849d580f455ff9b9c41fb"><span data-offset-key="a777183a3d7849d580f455ff9b9c41fb:0">Let’s take a look at them.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="302ae99eb88447f29eb769c3e525272e"><span data-offset-key="302ae99eb88447f29eb769c3e525272e:0">`​`</span></span></span>

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="dfe970dd718146e89122b8762bad253e"><span data-offset-key="dfe970dd718146e89122b8762bad253e:0">`​`</span></span></span>

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="d432130ea27b4053bde3b62d6cc5e9d4"><span data-offset-key="d432130ea27b4053bde3b62d6cc5e9d4:0">`collections.OrderedDict`</span><span data-offset-key="d432130ea27b4053bde3b62d6cc5e9d4:1">: Remember the Insertion Order of Keys</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="1050d8edee67465eb313ac86e905138e"><span data-offset-key="1050d8edee67465eb313ac86e905138e:0">Python includes a specialized </span><span data-offset-key="1050d8edee67465eb313ac86e905138e:1">`dict`</span><span data-offset-key="1050d8edee67465eb313ac86e905138e:2"> subclass that remembers the insertion order of keys added to it: </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-ordereddict/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="f598a27d9a824af1bcf167ffecf208fe"><span data-offset-key="f598a27d9a824af1bcf167ffecf208fe:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">collections.OrderedDict</code></span></span></a><span data-key="b025e173b5b74b10b3c17c0c104f687d"><span data-offset-key="b025e173b5b74b10b3c17c0c104f687d:0">.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="1d4d018d6c3247b0987b76bcc866df18"><span data-offset-key="1d4d018d6c3247b0987b76bcc866df18:0">**Note:**</span><span data-offset-key="1d4d018d6c3247b0987b76bcc866df18:1"> </span><span data-offset-key="1d4d018d6c3247b0987b76bcc866df18:2">`OrderedDict`</span><span data-offset-key="1d4d018d6c3247b0987b76bcc866df18:3"> is not a built-in part of the core language and must be imported from the </span><span data-offset-key="1d4d018d6c3247b0987b76bcc866df18:4">`collections`</span><span data-offset-key="1d4d018d6c3247b0987b76bcc866df18:5"> module in the standard library.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="90472f60fef048efbc59aa97deada0a6"><span data-offset-key="90472f60fef048efbc59aa97deada0a6:0">While standard </span><span data-offset-key="90472f60fef048efbc59aa97deada0a6:1">`dict`</span><span data-offset-key="90472f60fef048efbc59aa97deada0a6:2"> instances preserve the insertion order of keys in CPython 3.6 and above, this was simply a </span></span><a href="https://mail.python.org/pipermail/python-dev/2016-September/146327.html" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="e86447149acd4985acfe72382258ad38"><span data-offset-key="e86447149acd4985acfe72382258ad38:0">side effect</span></span></a><span data-key="308975df7de6468cb68fbcd362b45fd6"><span data-offset-key="308975df7de6468cb68fbcd362b45fd6:0"> of the CPython implementation and was not defined in the language spec until Python 3.7. So, if key order is important for your algorithm to work, then it’s best to communicate this clearly by explicitly using the </span><span data-offset-key="308975df7de6468cb68fbcd362b45fd6:1">`OrderedDict`</span><span data-offset-key="308975df7de6468cb68fbcd362b45fd6:2"> class:</span></span></span>

     import collections​ d = collections.OrderedDict(one=1, two=2, three=3)​​ dOrderedDict([('one', 1), ('two', 2), ('three', 3)])​​ d["four"] = 4​ dOrderedDict([('one', 1), ('two', 2), ('three', 3), ('four', 4)])​​ d.keys()odict_keys(['one', 'two', 'three', 'four'])` ​Until [Python 3.8](https://realpython.com/python38-new-features/), you couldn’t iterate over dictionary items in reverse order using `reversed()`. Only `OrderedDict` instances offered that functionality. Even in Python 3.8, `dict` and `OrderedDict` objects aren’t exactly the same. `OrderedDict` instances have a [`.move_to_end()` method](https://realpython.com/python-data-types/) that is unavailable on plain `dict` instance, as well as a more customizable [`.popitem()` method](https://docs.python.org/3/library/collections.html#collections.OrderedDict.popitem) than the one plain `dict` instances.

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="2bee064beba442fd945f1fedcfdf6c70"><span data-offset-key="2bee064beba442fd945f1fedcfdf6c70:0">`collections.defaultdict`</span><span data-offset-key="2bee064beba442fd945f1fedcfdf6c70:1">: Return Default Values for Missing Keys</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="044a459fe0e948cbb2f7574c9d3f14d6"><span data-offset-key="044a459fe0e948cbb2f7574c9d3f14d6:0">The </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/collections.html#collections.defaultdict" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="2c01c87a333b4b6e8beb799ec11f6688"><span data-offset-key="2c01c87a333b4b6e8beb799ec11f6688:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">defaultdict</code></span></span></a><span data-key="3afc588395dd4c4cabef514fdd19c84b"><span data-offset-key="3afc588395dd4c4cabef514fdd19c84b:0"> class is another dictionary subclass that accepts a callable in its constructor whose return value will be used if a requested key cannot be found.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="9ebfc5fc87354951bbf1f0cd61eccf33"><span data-offset-key="9ebfc5fc87354951bbf1f0cd61eccf33:0">This can save you some typing and make your intentions clearer as compared to using </span><span data-offset-key="9ebfc5fc87354951bbf1f0cd61eccf33:1">`get()`</span><span data-offset-key="9ebfc5fc87354951bbf1f0cd61eccf33:2"> or catching a </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-keyerror/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="0da748b3983e4e7eb401fb83478a7b59"><span data-offset-key="0da748b3983e4e7eb401fb83478a7b59:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">KeyError</code></span><span data-offset-key="0da748b3983e4e7eb401fb83478a7b59:1"> exception</span></span></a><span data-key="9eadaac56ee9479a96c24f0acea189e9"><span data-offset-key="9eadaac56ee9479a96c24f0acea189e9:0"> in regular dictionaries:</span></span></span>

     from collections import defaultdict​ dd = defaultdict(list)​​ # Accessing a missing key creates it and​ # initializes it using the default factory,​ # i.e. list() in this example:​ dd["dogs"].append("Rufus")​ dd["dogs"].append("Kathrin")​ dd["dogs"].append("Mr Sniffles")​​ dd["dogs"]['Rufus', 'Kathrin', 'Mr Sniffles']`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="12a5bcf7f15c49be86e784f66c7d4dcc"><span data-offset-key="12a5bcf7f15c49be86e784f66c7d4dcc:0">`collections.ChainMap`</span><span data-offset-key="12a5bcf7f15c49be86e784f66c7d4dcc:1">: Search Multiple Dictionaries as a Single Mapping</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="8e11fd33e71546f18d0ed414aa1a4bd6"><span data-offset-key="8e11fd33e71546f18d0ed414aa1a4bd6:0">The </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/collections.html#collections.ChainMap" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="452c6c0a30874afa8668899d6b5ca774"><span data-offset-key="452c6c0a30874afa8668899d6b5ca774:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">collections.ChainMap</code></span></span></a><span data-key="08c3bbba889a42178c7294c1d3a0b58a"><span data-offset-key="08c3bbba889a42178c7294c1d3a0b58a:0"> data structure groups multiple dictionaries into a single mapping. Lookups search the underlying mappings one by one until a key is found. Insertions, updates, and deletions only affect the first mapping added to the chain:</span></span></span>

     from collections import ChainMap​ dict1 = {"one": 1, "two": 2}​ dict2 = {"three": 3, "four": 4}​ chain = ChainMap(dict1, dict2)​​ chainChainMap({'one': 1, 'two': 2}, {'three': 3, 'four': 4})​​ # ChainMap searches each collection in the chain​ # from left to right until it finds the key (or fails):​ chain["three"]3​ chain["one"]1​ chain["missing"]Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>KeyError: 'missing'`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ed1ecf2b411344b8a83a1dfe1fde4f5b"><span data-offset-key="ed1ecf2b411344b8a83a1dfe1fde4f5b:0">`types.MappingProxyType`</span><span data-offset-key="ed1ecf2b411344b8a83a1dfe1fde4f5b:1">: A Wrapper for Making Read-Only Dictionaries</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="4289006f3ca44cb9a3f443f05ea46d64"><span data-offset-key="4289006f3ca44cb9a3f443f05ea46d64:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/types.html#types.MappingProxyType" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="229c023d7e714731ba9a0cb08fb55d97"><span data-offset-key="229c023d7e714731ba9a0cb08fb55d97:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">MappingProxyType</code></span></span></a><span data-key="cfe5c1680f3a44539100fd0806a21fea"><span data-offset-key="cfe5c1680f3a44539100fd0806a21fea:0"> is a wrapper around a standard dictionary that provides a read-only view into the wrapped dictionary’s data. This class was added in Python 3.3 and can be used to create immutable proxy versions of dictionaries.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="fab00627f1744a0badb1f1fcdcaf0367"><span data-offset-key="fab00627f1744a0badb1f1fcdcaf0367:0">` ``pyMappingProxyType `</span><span data-offset-key="fab00627f1744a0badb1f1fcdcaf0367:1"> can be helpful if, for example, you’d like to return a dictionary carrying internal state from a class or module while discouraging write access to this object. Using </span><span data-offset-key="fab00627f1744a0badb1f1fcdcaf0367:2">`MappingProxyType`</span><span data-offset-key="fab00627f1744a0badb1f1fcdcaf0367:3"> allows you to put these restrictions in place without first having to create a full copy of the dictionary:</span></span></span>

     from types import MappingProxyType​ writable = {"one": 1, "two": 2}​ read_only = MappingProxyType(writable)​​ # The proxy is read-only:​ read_only["one"]1​ read_only["one"] = 23Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: 'mappingproxy' object does not support item assignment​​ # Updates to the original are reflected in the proxy:​ writable["one"] = 42​ read_onlymappingproxy({'one': 42, 'two': 2})`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="8b8ed50dc0974d2f86f6f3c3e29bbbce"><span data-offset-key="8b8ed50dc0974d2f86f6f3c3e29bbbce:0">Dictionaries in Python: Summary</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="99ece570c4564624b4a01ec1597fd618"><span data-offset-key="99ece570c4564624b4a01ec1597fd618:0">All the Python dictionary implementations listed in this tutorial are valid implementations that are built into the Python standard library.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="441b3fea39a74c51ab71d1d3746a8377"><span data-offset-key="441b3fea39a74c51ab71d1d3746a8377:0">If you’re looking for a general recommendation on which mapping type to use in your programs, I’d point you to the built-in </span><span data-offset-key="441b3fea39a74c51ab71d1d3746a8377:1">`dict`</span><span data-offset-key="441b3fea39a74c51ab71d1d3746a8377:2"> data type. It’s a versatile and optimized hash table implementation that’s built directly into the core language.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="703939d5682843f5adb73a59ec38cabc"><span data-offset-key="703939d5682843f5adb73a59ec38cabc:0">I would recommend that you use one of the other data types listed here only if you have special requirements that go beyond what’s provided by </span><span data-offset-key="703939d5682843f5adb73a59ec38cabc:1">`dict`</span><span data-offset-key="703939d5682843f5adb73a59ec38cabc:2">.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ac16129e0ad540598eed7650bcb4c35e"><span data-offset-key="ac16129e0ad540598eed7650bcb4c35e:0">All the implementations are valid options, but your code will be clearer and easier to maintain if it relies on standard Python dictionaries most of the time.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH600-23f228db--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0ed83909ccda4109bd184fd016f5936e"><span data-offset-key="0ed83909ccda4109bd184fd016f5936e:0">Array Data Structures</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="850450e1b9d54b3eb1b81af752cafc15"><span data-offset-key="850450e1b9d54b3eb1b81af752cafc15:0">An </span><span data-offset-key="850450e1b9d54b3eb1b81af752cafc15:1">**array**</span><span data-offset-key="850450e1b9d54b3eb1b81af752cafc15:2"> is a fundamental data structure available in most programming languages, and it has a wide range of uses across different algorithms.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ca8bfcaf83584866b8cb64544be696c9"><span data-offset-key="ca8bfcaf83584866b8cb64544be696c9:0">In this section, you’ll take a look at array implementations in Python that use only core language features or functionality that’s included in the Python standard library. You’ll see the strengths and weaknesses of each approach so you can decide which implementation is right for your use case.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0c5bc615e24a42beb2e13491ffd6f093"><span data-offset-key="0c5bc615e24a42beb2e13491ffd6f093:0">But before we jump in, let’s cover some of the basics first. How do arrays work, and what are they used for? Arrays consist of fixed-size data records that allow each element to be efficiently located based on its index:</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0311baccb08d437fa635ccd1a908e8ae"><span data-offset-key="0311baccb08d437fa635ccd1a908e8ae:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><a href="https://files.realpython.com/media/python-linked-list-array-visualization.5b9f4c4040cb.jpeg" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="60f85746fa5848f396958e1722db2b26"><span data-offset-key="60f85746fa5848f396958e1722db2b26:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><span data-slate-void="true" data-key="ba4194bfdf0f451eb63ccdd54ee0b993"><span><span class="reset-3c756112--frameInlineVoid-73b6a96b" data-key="ba4194bfdf0f451eb63ccdd54ee0b993"><img src="https://files.realpython.com/media/python-linked-list-array-visualization.5b9f4c4040cb.jpeg" alt="Visual representation of an array" class="image-67b14f24--image-54d049be" /></span></span></span><span data-key="0973277b85714009a441bfff80ca48a8"><span data-offset-key="0973277b85714009a441bfff80ca48a8:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span></a><span data-key="aca7c4c155824039a11e09f2cef6ef2f"><span data-offset-key="aca7c4c155824039a11e09f2cef6ef2f:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="2cf3eae83b4d4648901806bbb7dce349"><span data-offset-key="2cf3eae83b4d4648901806bbb7dce349:0">Because arrays store information in adjoining blocks of memory, they’re considered </span><span data-offset-key="2cf3eae83b4d4648901806bbb7dce349:1">**contiguous**</span><span data-offset-key="2cf3eae83b4d4648901806bbb7dce349:2"> data structures (as opposed to </span><span data-offset-key="2cf3eae83b4d4648901806bbb7dce349:3">**linked**</span><span data-offset-key="2cf3eae83b4d4648901806bbb7dce349:4"> data structures like linked lists, for example).</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="aa9dc0ea6f72444eac18d3d223ef64e4"><span data-offset-key="aa9dc0ea6f72444eac18d3d223ef64e4:0">A real-world analogy for an array data structure is a parking lot. You can look at the parking lot as a whole and treat it as a single object, but inside the lot there are parking spots indexed by a unique number. Parking spots are containers for vehicles—each parking spot can either be empty or have a car, a motorbike, or some other vehicle parked on it.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="56ad970451b94c66b703fcb69a787ee8"><span data-offset-key="56ad970451b94c66b703fcb69a787ee8:0">But not all parking lots are the same. Some parking lots may be restricted to only one type of vehicle. For example, a motor home parking lot wouldn’t allow bikes to be parked on it. A restricted parking lot corresponds to a </span><span data-offset-key="56ad970451b94c66b703fcb69a787ee8:1">**typed**</span><span data-offset-key="56ad970451b94c66b703fcb69a787ee8:2"> array data structure that allows only elements that have the same data type stored in them.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="3575af7dfbd2403aac554ef9ad4b9394"><span data-offset-key="3575af7dfbd2403aac554ef9ad4b9394:0">Performance-wise, it’s very fast to look up an element contained in an array given the element’s index. A proper array implementation guarantees a constant </span><span data-offset-key="3575af7dfbd2403aac554ef9ad4b9394:1">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="3575af7dfbd2403aac554ef9ad4b9394:2">(1) access time for this case.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e1dcac81203646c3940e1b5b6ac737d4"><span data-offset-key="e1dcac81203646c3940e1b5b6ac737d4:0">Python includes several array-like data structures in its standard library that each have slightly different characteristics. Let’s take a look.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH600-23f228db--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="6b470e4cdd714f81a97ad6713a21c4cd"><span data-offset-key="6b470e4cdd714f81a97ad6713a21c4cd:0">`list`</span><span data-offset-key="6b470e4cdd714f81a97ad6713a21c4cd:1">: Mutable Dynamic Arrays</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="7b42c2692d85443b953c75c5df350cfc"><span data-offset-key="7b42c2692d85443b953c75c5df350cfc:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3.6/library/stdtypes.html#lists" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="ba9fef54e65a433e8330ea6163c010bc"><span data-offset-key="ba9fef54e65a433e8330ea6163c010bc:0">Lists</span></span></a><span data-key="24f7210829644dc99739ec492e5dabe1"><span data-offset-key="24f7210829644dc99739ec492e5dabe1:0"> are a part of the core Python language. Despite their name, Python’s lists are implemented as </span><span data-offset-key="24f7210829644dc99739ec492e5dabe1:1">**dynamic arrays**</span><span data-offset-key="24f7210829644dc99739ec492e5dabe1:2"> behind the scenes.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="f0ee4660aaf648d0bd41f69128bb66f2"><span data-offset-key="f0ee4660aaf648d0bd41f69128bb66f2:0">This means a list allows elements to be added or removed, and the list will automatically adjust the backing store that holds these elements by allocating or releasing memory.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="610c3d37f1c64c9296d161d2b121285e"><span data-offset-key="610c3d37f1c64c9296d161d2b121285e:0">Python lists can hold arbitrary elements—everything is an object in Python, including functions. Therefore, you can mix and match different kinds of data types and store them all in a single list.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="d12a2e99fb1f4686a7b203c7c6443e82"><span data-offset-key="d12a2e99fb1f4686a7b203c7c6443e82:0">This can be a powerful feature, but the downside is that supporting multiple data types at the same time means that data is generally less tightly packed. As a result, the whole structure takes up more space:</span></span></span>

    ​​ arr = ["one", "two", "three"]​ arr[0]'one'​​ # Lists have a nice repr:​ arr['one', 'two', 'three']​​ # Lists are mutable:​ arr[1] = "hello"​ arr['one', 'hello', 'three']​​ del arr[1]​ arr['one', 'three']​​ # Lists can hold arbitrary data types:​ arr.append(23)​ arr['one', 'three', 23]`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="df4ce71aac1e474ca9843096743ccede"><span data-offset-key="df4ce71aac1e474ca9843096743ccede:0">`tuple`</span><span data-offset-key="df4ce71aac1e474ca9843096743ccede:1">: Immutable Containers</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="35cd563b5b8d4745acfdef91051f8f6c"><span data-offset-key="35cd563b5b8d4745acfdef91051f8f6c:0">Just like lists, </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#tuple" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="6bab0cb89f7d48118242a494566b9aa0"><span data-offset-key="6bab0cb89f7d48118242a494566b9aa0:0">tuples</span></span></a><span data-key="a62bc576fbca43ecb0172294de193b2a"><span data-offset-key="a62bc576fbca43ecb0172294de193b2a:0"> are part of the Python core language. Unlike lists, however, Python’s </span><span data-offset-key="a62bc576fbca43ecb0172294de193b2a:1">`tuple`</span><span data-offset-key="a62bc576fbca43ecb0172294de193b2a:2"> objects are immutable. This means elements can’t be added or removed dynamically—all elements in a tuple must be defined at creation time.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="f45c6d6e23e8444b8eee259f42226878"><span data-offset-key="f45c6d6e23e8444b8eee259f42226878:0">Tuples are another data structure that can hold elements of arbitrary data types. Having this flexibility is powerful, but again, it also means that data is less tightly packed than it would be in a typed array:</span></span></span>

     arr = ("one", "two", "three")​ arr[0]'one'​​ # Tuples have a nice repr:​ arr('one', 'two', 'three')​​ # Tuples are immutable:​ arr[1] = "hello"Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: 'tuple' object does not support item assignment​​ del arr[1]Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: 'tuple' object doesn't support item deletion​​ # Tuples can hold arbitrary data types:​ # (Adding elements creates a copy of the tuple)​ arr + (23,)('one', 'two', 'three', 23)` ```

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="f9f5b305d2514f13a97e0f757a08d692"><span data-offset-key="f9f5b305d2514f13a97e0f757a08d692:0">`array.array`</span><span data-offset-key="f9f5b305d2514f13a97e0f757a08d692:1">: Basic Typed Arrays</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ff290c5699c44cc2abfdf5277062e9a9"><span data-offset-key="ff290c5699c44cc2abfdf5277062e9a9:0">Python’s </span><span data-offset-key="ff290c5699c44cc2abfdf5277062e9a9:1">`array`</span><span data-offset-key="ff290c5699c44cc2abfdf5277062e9a9:2"> module provides space-efficient storage of basic C-style data types like bytes, 32-bit integers, floating-point numbers, and so on.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e499eae7d53a4e77ba9359474dfdfe8a"><span data-offset-key="e499eae7d53a4e77ba9359474dfdfe8a:0">Arrays created with the </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/array.html" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="61a9c1c3fc664cae8876e479a8a31365"><span data-offset-key="61a9c1c3fc664cae8876e479a8a31365:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">array.array</code></span></span></a><span data-key="321274ffbae14cf4ad51b4e6a8ea9580"><span data-offset-key="321274ffbae14cf4ad51b4e6a8ea9580:0"> class are mutable and behave similarly to lists except for one important difference: they’re </span><span data-offset-key="321274ffbae14cf4ad51b4e6a8ea9580:1">**typed arrays**</span><span data-offset-key="321274ffbae14cf4ad51b4e6a8ea9580:2"> constrained to a single data type.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="5c39b5b4f9954ab1af4b8b16627cd77d"><span data-offset-key="5c39b5b4f9954ab1af4b8b16627cd77d:0">Because of this constraint, </span><span data-offset-key="5c39b5b4f9954ab1af4b8b16627cd77d:1">`array.array`</span><span data-offset-key="5c39b5b4f9954ab1af4b8b16627cd77d:2"> objects with many elements are more space efficient than lists and tuples. The elements stored in them are tightly packed, and this can be useful if you need to store many elements of the same type.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="a892643f699d4381809fc518b271613a"><span data-offset-key="a892643f699d4381809fc518b271613a:0">Also, arrays support many of the same methods as regular lists, and you might be able to use them as a drop-in replacement without requiring other changes to your application code.</span></span></span>

     import array​ arr = array.array("f", (1.0, 1.5, 2.0, 2.5))​ arr[1]1.5​​ # Arrays have a nice repr:​ arrarray('f', [1.0, 1.5, 2.0, 2.5])​​ # Arrays are mutable:​ arr[1] = 23.0​ arrarray('f', [1.0, 23.0, 2.0, 2.5])​​ del arr[1]​ arrarray('f', [1.0, 2.0, 2.5])​​ arr.append(42.0)​ arrarray('f', [1.0, 2.0, 2.5, 42.0])​​ # Arrays are "typed":​ arr[1] = "hello"Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: must be real number, not str` ```

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="8f427a39d1a34f689ca1a5f52adcb6f5"><span data-offset-key="8f427a39d1a34f689ca1a5f52adcb6f5:0">`str`</span><span data-offset-key="8f427a39d1a34f689ca1a5f52adcb6f5:1">: Immutable Arrays of Unicode Characters</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ea7e37ba03a7471987eb8dd25dc45dbe"><span data-offset-key="ea7e37ba03a7471987eb8dd25dc45dbe:0">Python 3.x uses </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#text-sequence-type-str" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="72084db0f7a04ba6b14e90d0b7ae4be2"><span data-offset-key="72084db0f7a04ba6b14e90d0b7ae4be2:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">str</code></span></span></a><span data-key="82da92a6a3d54ddc9580eb9deef6e671"><span data-offset-key="82da92a6a3d54ddc9580eb9deef6e671:0"> objects to store textual data as immutable sequences of </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-encodings-guide/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="eaf4a07358b84ea68315b1e13afe882a"><span data-offset-key="eaf4a07358b84ea68315b1e13afe882a:0">Unicode characters</span></span></a><span data-key="157e330c80414dd480b5ec61bebd1630"><span data-offset-key="157e330c80414dd480b5ec61bebd1630:0">. Practically speaking, that means a </span><span data-offset-key="157e330c80414dd480b5ec61bebd1630:1">`str`</span><span data-offset-key="157e330c80414dd480b5ec61bebd1630:2"> is an immutable array of characters. Oddly enough, it’s also a </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-thinking-recursively/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="da86d179a7dc41bfb5062e0fa8ded285"><span data-offset-key="da86d179a7dc41bfb5062e0fa8ded285:0">recursive</span></span></a><span data-key="fcb9b142cafb47c48c3e2a7407b0592f"><span data-offset-key="fcb9b142cafb47c48c3e2a7407b0592f:0"> data structure—each character in a string is itself a </span><span data-offset-key="fcb9b142cafb47c48c3e2a7407b0592f:1">`str`</span><span data-offset-key="fcb9b142cafb47c48c3e2a7407b0592f:2"> object of length 1.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="b6664c727f9c431ba87a99be5385b122"><span data-offset-key="b6664c727f9c431ba87a99be5385b122:0">String objects are space efficient because they’re tightly packed and they specialize in a single data type. If you’re storing Unicode text, then you should use a string.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="2ba7d85168e14ddc8bdc046bbcca5cdb"><span data-offset-key="2ba7d85168e14ddc8bdc046bbcca5cdb:0">Because strings are immutable in Python, modifying a string requires creating a modified copy. The closest equivalent to a mutable string is storing individual characters inside a list:</span></span></span>

     arr = "abcd"​ arr[1]'b'​​ arr'abcd'​​ # Strings are immutable:​ arr[1] = "e"Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: 'str' object does not support item assignment​​ del arr[1]Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: 'str' object doesn't support item deletion​​ # Strings can be unpacked into a list to​ # get a mutable representation:​ list("abcd")['a', 'b', 'c', 'd']​ "".join(list("abcd"))'abcd'​​ # Strings are recursive data structures:​ type("abc")"<class 'str'>"​ type("abc"[0])"<class 'str'>"` ```

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="258957db93984e52bf33fa79f20bb562"><span data-offset-key="258957db93984e52bf33fa79f20bb562:0">`bytes`</span><span data-offset-key="258957db93984e52bf33fa79f20bb562:1">: Immutable Arrays of Single Bytes</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="49fdb0f7891b4d9ba11eace6756f329b"><span data-offset-key="49fdb0f7891b4d9ba11eace6756f329b:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#bytes-objects" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="b800829f77db416a920b649a0dc18b6f"><span data-offset-key="b800829f77db416a920b649a0dc18b6f:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">bytes</code></span></span></a><span data-key="c5d15448964643f99fcac32620dd10e6"><span data-offset-key="c5d15448964643f99fcac32620dd10e6:0"> objects are immutable sequences of single bytes, or integers in the range 0 ≤ </span><span data-offset-key="c5d15448964643f99fcac32620dd10e6:1">_x_</span><span data-offset-key="c5d15448964643f99fcac32620dd10e6:2"> ≤ 255. Conceptually, </span><span data-offset-key="c5d15448964643f99fcac32620dd10e6:3">`bytes`</span><span data-offset-key="c5d15448964643f99fcac32620dd10e6:4"> objects are similar to </span><span data-offset-key="c5d15448964643f99fcac32620dd10e6:5">`str`</span><span data-offset-key="c5d15448964643f99fcac32620dd10e6:6"> objects, and you can also think of them as immutable arrays of bytes.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="2f439a618bb84d65946a5b5d2663e1a6"><span data-offset-key="2f439a618bb84d65946a5b5d2663e1a6:0">Like strings, </span><span data-offset-key="2f439a618bb84d65946a5b5d2663e1a6:1">`bytes`</span><span data-offset-key="2f439a618bb84d65946a5b5d2663e1a6:2"> have their own literal syntax for creating objects and are space efficient. </span><span data-offset-key="2f439a618bb84d65946a5b5d2663e1a6:3">`bytes`</span><span data-offset-key="2f439a618bb84d65946a5b5d2663e1a6:4"> objects are immutable, but unlike strings, there’s a dedicated mutable byte array data type called </span><span data-offset-key="2f439a618bb84d65946a5b5d2663e1a6:5">`bytearray`</span><span data-offset-key="2f439a618bb84d65946a5b5d2663e1a6:6"> that they can be unpacked into:</span></span></span>

     arr = bytes((0, 1, 2, 3))​ arr[1]1​​ # Bytes literals have their own syntax:​ arrb'\x00\x01\x02\x03'​ arr = b"\x00\x01\x02\x03"​​ # Only valid `bytes` are allowed:​ bytes((0, 300))Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>ValueError: bytes must be in range(0, 256)​​ # Bytes are immutable:​ arr[1] = 23Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: 'bytes' object does not support item assignment​​ del arr[1]Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: 'bytes' object doesn't support item deletion``

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="8d8a4e76deb241d4a540f35f60a98f7f"><span data-offset-key="8d8a4e76deb241d4a540f35f60a98f7f:0">`bytearray`</span><span data-offset-key="8d8a4e76deb241d4a540f35f60a98f7f:1">: Mutable Arrays of Single Bytes</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="bffb5c45e28847cb95bb98fdaf2ecd3d"><span data-offset-key="bffb5c45e28847cb95bb98fdaf2ecd3d:0">The </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3.1/library/functions.html#bytearray" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="a3beaaefb8364d08a153379a9c5b8ce5"><span data-offset-key="a3beaaefb8364d08a153379a9c5b8ce5:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">bytearray</code></span></span></a><span data-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9"><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:0"> type is a mutable sequence of integers in the range 0 ≤ </span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:1">_x_</span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:2"> ≤ 255. The </span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:3">`bytearray`</span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:4"> object is closely related to the </span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:5">`bytes`</span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:6"> object, with the main difference being that a </span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:7">`bytearray`</span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:8"> can be modified freely—you can overwrite elements, remove existing elements, or add new ones. The </span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:9">`bytearray`</span><span data-offset-key="4fe27950be834be3b23169c2045473b9:10"> object will grow and shrink accordingly.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff"><span data-offset-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff:0">A </span><span data-offset-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff:1">`bytearray`</span><span data-offset-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff:2"> can be converted back into immutable </span><span data-offset-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff:3">`bytes`</span><span data-offset-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff:4"> objects, but this involves copying the stored data in full—a slow operation taking </span><span data-offset-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff:5">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff:6">(</span><span data-offset-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff:7">_n_</span><span data-offset-key="61884e076df64ae38249d83b55d956ff:8">) time:</span></span></span>

     arr = bytearray((0, 1, 2, 3))​ arr[1]1​​ # The bytearray repr:​ arrbytearray(b'\x00\x01\x02\x03')​​ # Bytearrays are mutable:​ arr[1] = 23​ arrbytearray(b'\x00\x17\x02\x03')​​ arr[1]23​​ # Bytearrays can grow and shrink in size:​ del arr[1]​ arrbytearray(b'\x00\x02\x03')​​ arr.append(42)​ arrbytearray(b'\x00\x02\x03*')​​ # Bytearrays can only hold `bytes`​ # (integers in the range 0 <= x <= 255)​ arr[1] = "hello"Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: 'str' object cannot be interpreted as an integer​​ arr[1] = 300Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>ValueError: byte must be in range(0, 256)​​ # Bytearrays can be converted back into bytes objects:​ # (This will copy the data)​ bytes(arr)b'\x00\x02\x03*'``

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="14b1d1b09f444e939b0d232e0c2f4c3f"><span data-offset-key="14b1d1b09f444e939b0d232e0c2f4c3f:0">Arrays in Python: Summary</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ab2c3035a6c2486cb9f1c6af8d0a9501"><span data-offset-key="ab2c3035a6c2486cb9f1c6af8d0a9501:0">There are a number of built-in data structures you can choose from when it comes to implementing arrays in Python. In this section, you’ve focused on core language features and data structures included in the standard library.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="3614616585704762b67e35014a40062f"><span data-offset-key="3614616585704762b67e35014a40062f:0">If you’re willing to go beyond the Python standard library, then third-party packages like </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/numpy-array-programming/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="5c3aa25711a548f8ad1a7f0ea97c4773"><span data-offset-key="5c3aa25711a548f8ad1a7f0ea97c4773:0">NumPy</span></span></a><span data-key="9180d475aa80427085f6f74730e7d543"><span data-offset-key="9180d475aa80427085f6f74730e7d543:0"> and </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/pandas-dataframe/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="ba8615b9aaeb43e0b122000a2b5f878b"><span data-offset-key="ba8615b9aaeb43e0b122000a2b5f878b:0">pandas</span></span></a><span data-key="c523b0be71d94492a1185f09ad703d5d"><span data-offset-key="c523b0be71d94492a1185f09ad703d5d:0"> offer a wide range of fast array implementations for scientific computing and data science.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="a16ff9ccf6c34002bfc6a0dbfdd02921"><span data-offset-key="a16ff9ccf6c34002bfc6a0dbfdd02921:0">If you want to restrict yourself to the array data structures included with Python, then here are a few guidelines:</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="c9b7fbb97de8479c9994e3d8737cb1a4"><span data-offset-key="c9b7fbb97de8479c9994e3d8737cb1a4:0">If you need to store arbitrary objects, potentially with mixed data types, then use a </span><span data-offset-key="c9b7fbb97de8479c9994e3d8737cb1a4:1">`list`</span><span data-offset-key="c9b7fbb97de8479c9994e3d8737cb1a4:2"> or a </span><span data-offset-key="c9b7fbb97de8479c9994e3d8737cb1a4:3">`tuple`</span><span data-offset-key="c9b7fbb97de8479c9994e3d8737cb1a4:4">, depending on whether or not you want an immutable data structure.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="06168bddf3464618a3398874a9fce45a"><span data-offset-key="06168bddf3464618a3398874a9fce45a:0">If you have numeric (integer or floating-point) data and tight packing and performance is important, then try out </span><span data-offset-key="06168bddf3464618a3398874a9fce45a:1">`array.array`</span><span data-offset-key="06168bddf3464618a3398874a9fce45a:2">.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="5205ee1e57454bc2866cd0d13c4bedfa"><span data-offset-key="5205ee1e57454bc2866cd0d13c4bedfa:0">If you have textual data represented as Unicode characters, then use Python’s built-in </span><span data-offset-key="5205ee1e57454bc2866cd0d13c4bedfa:1">`str`</span><span data-offset-key="5205ee1e57454bc2866cd0d13c4bedfa:2">. If you need a mutable string-like data structure, then use a </span><span data-offset-key="5205ee1e57454bc2866cd0d13c4bedfa:3">`list`</span><span data-offset-key="5205ee1e57454bc2866cd0d13c4bedfa:4"> of characters.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="135eb5103ec14a6d89d22dcb1dda5cf6"><span data-offset-key="135eb5103ec14a6d89d22dcb1dda5cf6:0">If you want to store a contiguous block of bytes, then use the immutable </span><span data-offset-key="135eb5103ec14a6d89d22dcb1dda5cf6:1">`bytes`</span><span data-offset-key="135eb5103ec14a6d89d22dcb1dda5cf6:2"> type or a </span><span data-offset-key="135eb5103ec14a6d89d22dcb1dda5cf6:3">`bytearray`</span><span data-offset-key="135eb5103ec14a6d89d22dcb1dda5cf6:4"> if you need a mutable data structure.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="6541ec0786a042e7983a1dbc64f6b310"><span data-offset-key="6541ec0786a042e7983a1dbc64f6b310:0">In most cases, I like to start out with a simple </span><span data-offset-key="6541ec0786a042e7983a1dbc64f6b310:1">`list`</span><span data-offset-key="6541ec0786a042e7983a1dbc64f6b310:2">. I’ll only specialize later on if performance or storage space becomes an issue. Most of the time, using a general-purpose array data structure like </span><span data-offset-key="6541ec0786a042e7983a1dbc64f6b310:3">`list`</span><span data-offset-key="6541ec0786a042e7983a1dbc64f6b310:4"> gives you the fastest development speed and the most programming convenience.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="06fa2798cbf841fb989b50ee92a66c6e"><span data-offset-key="06fa2798cbf841fb989b50ee92a66c6e:0">I’ve found that this is usually much more important in the beginning than trying to squeeze out every last drop of performance right from the start.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH600-23f228db--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="55946e380a7a439d9d9cf6ef37075eff"><span data-offset-key="55946e380a7a439d9d9cf6ef37075eff:0">Records, Structs, and Data Transfer Objects</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="967437a0fdb3402b82a9e31a40e94afe"><span data-offset-key="967437a0fdb3402b82a9e31a40e94afe:0">Compared to arrays, </span><span data-offset-key="967437a0fdb3402b82a9e31a40e94afe:1">**record**</span><span data-offset-key="967437a0fdb3402b82a9e31a40e94afe:2"> data structures provide a fixed number of fields. Each field can have a name and may also have a different type.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="d712b5096c4940a8bbfd6412577dab23"><span data-offset-key="d712b5096c4940a8bbfd6412577dab23:0">In this section, you’ll see how to implement records, structs, and plain old data objects in Python using only built-in data types and classes from the standard library.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="3baf6e747123402887ee018ee474af46"><span data-offset-key="3baf6e747123402887ee018ee474af46:0">**Note:**</span><span data-offset-key="3baf6e747123402887ee018ee474af46:1"> I’m using the definition of a record loosely here. For example, I’m also going to discuss types like Python’s built-in </span><span data-offset-key="3baf6e747123402887ee018ee474af46:2">`tuple`</span><span data-offset-key="3baf6e747123402887ee018ee474af46:3"> that may or may not be considered records in a strict sense because they don’t provide named fields.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="18a3e294bb1a4b5792736bc415e3b3a2"><span data-offset-key="18a3e294bb1a4b5792736bc415e3b3a2:0">Python offers several data types that you can use to implement records, structs, and data transfer objects. In this section, you’ll get a quick look at each implementation and its unique characteristics. At the end, you’ll find a summary and a decision-making guide that will help you make your own picks.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="39c98320678540079afab84544b880bb"><span data-offset-key="39c98320678540079afab84544b880bb:0">**Note:**</span><span data-offset-key="39c98320678540079afab84544b880bb:1"> This tutorial is adapted from the chapter “Common Data Structures in Python” in </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/products/python-tricks-book/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="09b7f10dfe714b0ea929b1f7b07da558"><span data-offset-key="09b7f10dfe714b0ea929b1f7b07da558:0"><em>Python Tricks: The Book</em></span></span></a><span data-key="1d28f84d89f046989ec31ea866d180fa"><span data-offset-key="1d28f84d89f046989ec31ea866d180fa:0">. If you enjoy what you’re reading, then be sure to check out </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/products/python-tricks-book/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="f2dd2adfa4b8444bb5b100dbaca64407"><span data-offset-key="f2dd2adfa4b8444bb5b100dbaca64407:0">the rest of the book</span></span></a><span data-key="9727969446d845d99840d6d9a26e501c"><span data-offset-key="9727969446d845d99840d6d9a26e501c:0">.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="85a5e0c2b0c548afb114c91eacdc25ba"><span data-offset-key="85a5e0c2b0c548afb114c91eacdc25ba:0">Alright, let’s get started!</span></span></span>

    ### `dict`: Simple Data Objects[](https://realpython.com/python-data-structures/#dict-simple-data-objects "Permanent link")​As mentioned [previously](https://realpython.com/python-data-structures/#dictionaries-maps-and-hash-tables), Python dictionaries store an arbitrary number of objects, each identified by a unique key. Dictionaries are also often called **maps** or **associative arrays** and allow for efficient lookup, insertion, and deletion of any object associated with a given key.​Using dictionaries as a record data type or data object in Python is possible. Dictionaries are easy to create in Python as they have their own syntactic sugar built into the language in the form of **dictionary literals**. The dictionary syntax is concise and quite convenient to type.​Data objects created using dictionaries are mutable, and there’s little protection against misspelled field names as fields can be added and removed freely at any time. Both of these properties can introduce surprising bugs, and there’s always a trade-off to be made between convenience and error resilience:​​​​```py car1 = {     "color": "red",     "mileage": 3812.4,     "automatic": True, }​ car2 = {     "color": "blue",     "mileage": 40231,     "automatic": False, }​​ # Dicts have a nice repr:​ car2{'color': 'blue', 'automatic': False, 'mileage': 40231}​​ # Get mileage:​ car2["mileage"]40231​​ # Dicts are mutable:​ car2["mileage"] = 12​ car2["windshield"] = "broken"​ car2{'windshield': 'broken', 'color': 'blue', 'automatic': False, 'mileage': 12}​​ # No protection against wrong field names,​ # or missing/extra fields:​ car3 = {     "colr": "green",     "automatic": False,     "windshield": "broken", }`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e7b300deab644f4ea3817ef55c88a1b9"><span data-offset-key="e7b300deab644f4ea3817ef55c88a1b9:0">`tuple`</span><span data-offset-key="e7b300deab644f4ea3817ef55c88a1b9:1">: Immutable Groups of Objects</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e14b9d102bc7466ea9875ae392613047"><span data-offset-key="e14b9d102bc7466ea9875ae392613047:0">Python’s tuples are a straightforward data structure for grouping arbitrary objects. Tuples are immutable—they can’t be modified once they’ve been created.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="7ec02f10ac5c444798b15ca3136f8271"><span data-offset-key="7ec02f10ac5c444798b15ca3136f8271:0">Performance-wise, tuples take up </span></span><a href="https://github.com/python/cpython/blob/1a5856bf9295fa73995898d576e0bedf016aee1f/Include/tupleobject.h#L10-L34" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="e3cdeb09b7694c20a1b4f1b847f1aeff"><span data-offset-key="e3cdeb09b7694c20a1b4f1b847f1aeff:0">slightly less memory</span></span></a><span data-key="2fc63f8ea62349cdb437ba314a8c4982"><span data-offset-key="2fc63f8ea62349cdb437ba314a8c4982:0"> than </span></span><a href="https://github.com/python/cpython/blob/b879fe82e7e5c3f7673c9a7fa4aad42bd05445d8/Include/listobject.h#L4-L41" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="7295071707a24c2197ed5c0609c5e9e1"><span data-offset-key="7295071707a24c2197ed5c0609c5e9e1:0">lists in CPython</span></span></a><span data-key="a32c959c89634e609829f2446fa82e16"><span data-offset-key="a32c959c89634e609829f2446fa82e16:0">, and they’re also faster to construct.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="aaa723472d7743b6b04c1e76c26f85e6"><span data-offset-key="aaa723472d7743b6b04c1e76c26f85e6:0">As you can see in the bytecode disassembly below, constructing a tuple constant takes a single </span><span data-offset-key="aaa723472d7743b6b04c1e76c26f85e6:1">`LOAD_CONST`</span><span data-offset-key="aaa723472d7743b6b04c1e76c26f85e6:2"> opcode, while constructing a list object with the same contents requires several more operations:</span></span></span>

     import dis​ dis.dis(compile("(23, 'a', 'b', 'c')", "", "eval")) 0 LOAD_CONST           4 ((23, "a", "b", "c")) 3 RETURN_VALUE​​ dis.dis(compile("[23, 'a', 'b', 'c']", "", "eval")) 0 LOAD_CONST           0 (23) 3 LOAD_CONST           1 ('a') 6 LOAD_CONST           2 ('b') 9 LOAD_CONST           3 ('c') 12 BUILD_LIST           4 15 RETURN_VALUE` ​However, you shouldn’t place too much emphasis on these differences. In practice, the performance difference will often be negligible, and trying to squeeze extra performance out of a program by switching from lists to tuples will likely be the wrong approach.​A potential downside of plain tuples is that the data you store in them can only be pulled out by accessing it through integer indexes. You can’t give names to individual properties stored in a tuple. This can impact code readability.​Also, a tuple is always an ad-hoc structure: it’s difficult to ensure that two tuples have the same number of fields and the same properties stored in them.​This makes it easy to introduce slip-of-the-mind bugs, such as mixing up the field order. Therefore, I would recommend that you keep the number of fields stored in a tuple as low as possible:​​​​```py # Fields: color, mileage, automatic​ car1 = ("red", 3812.4, True)​ car2 = ("blue", 40231.0, False)​​ # Tuple instances have a nice repr:​ car1('red', 3812.4, True)​ car2('blue', 40231.0, False)​​ # Get mileage:​ car2[1]40231.0​​ # Tuples are immutable:​ car2[1] = 12Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>TypeError: 'tuple' object does not support item assignment​​ # No protection against missing or extra fields​ # or a wrong order:​ car3 = (3431.5, "green", True, "silver")`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="17e2e823690c484e890175a6e13eb971"><span data-offset-key="17e2e823690c484e890175a6e13eb971:0">Write a Custom Class: More Work, More Control</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ddbe0b985a0d44d19d7fd788dc1e9a54"><span data-offset-key="ddbe0b985a0d44d19d7fd788dc1e9a54:0">**Classes**</span><span data-offset-key="ddbe0b985a0d44d19d7fd788dc1e9a54:1"> allow you to define reusable blueprints for data objects to ensure each object provides the same set of fields.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e872bb94707c4a34b798c46042c35b03"><span data-offset-key="e872bb94707c4a34b798c46042c35b03:0">Using regular Python classes as record data types is feasible, but it also takes manual work to get the convenience features of other implementations. For example, adding new fields to the </span><span data-offset-key="e872bb94707c4a34b798c46042c35b03:1">`__init__`</span><span data-offset-key="e872bb94707c4a34b798c46042c35b03:2"> constructor is verbose and takes time.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="280baafbe69e4f439130b9a08602b180"><span data-offset-key="280baafbe69e4f439130b9a08602b180:0">Also, the default string representation for objects instantiated from custom classes isn’t very helpful. To fix that, you may have to add your own </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3.4/reference/datamodel.html#object.__repr__" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="eee4abe30904408c9e3af54a407b417d"><span data-offset-key="eee4abe30904408c9e3af54a407b417d:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">**repr**</code></span></span></a><span data-key="c94cb6b3a087450a8142f1f7bd58ed1c"><span data-offset-key="c94cb6b3a087450a8142f1f7bd58ed1c:0"> method, which again is usually quite verbose and must be updated each time you add a new field.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="d08621f7ff3b4263827b2b3e337f85d2"><span data-offset-key="d08621f7ff3b4263827b2b3e337f85d2:0">Fields stored on classes are mutable, and new fields can be added freely, which you may or may not like. It’s possible to provide more access control and to create read-only fields using the </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/functions.html#property" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="1145f7691abe455ca62502b6f994612c"><span data-offset-key="1145f7691abe455ca62502b6f994612c:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">@property</code></span></span></a><span data-key="6ca22e7e88c04ce2a54889ee61c1b4ee"><span data-offset-key="6ca22e7e88c04ce2a54889ee61c1b4ee:0"> decorator, but once again, this requires writing more glue code.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0209fa20123844549f9ccc335cf450c4"><span data-offset-key="0209fa20123844549f9ccc335cf450c4:0">Writing a custom class is a great option whenever you’d like to add business logic and behavior to your record objects using methods. However, this means that these objects are technically no longer plain data objects:</span></span></span>

     class Car:     def __init__(self, color, mileage, automatic):         self.color = color         self.mileage = mileage         self.automatic = automatic​​ car1 = Car("red", 3812.4, True)​ car2 = Car("blue", 40231.0, False)​​ # Get the mileage:​ car2.mileage40231.0​​ # Classes are mutable:​ car2.mileage = 12​ car2.windshield = "broken"​​ # String representation is not very useful​ # (must add a manually written __repr__ method):​ car1<Car object at 0x1081e69e8>`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="4c64fdadf8e84dcda8b2dc65ac174dba"><span data-offset-key="4c64fdadf8e84dcda8b2dc65ac174dba:0">`dataclasses.dataclass`</span><span data-offset-key="4c64fdadf8e84dcda8b2dc65ac174dba:1">: Python 3.7+ Data Classes</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="bbdd9bc830114c738e7a51774ed52835"><span data-offset-key="bbdd9bc830114c738e7a51774ed52835:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-data-classes/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="b8a3058409bb4751a749bfd4148c4b1d"><span data-offset-key="b8a3058409bb4751a749bfd4148c4b1d:0">Data classes</span></span></a><span data-key="c6c50f1e17544e27bc9f97e115586bfa"><span data-offset-key="c6c50f1e17544e27bc9f97e115586bfa:0"> are available in Python 3.7 and above. They provide an excellent alternative to defining your own data storage classes from scratch.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="a0f7a86b7abc475ab7f548a840afaa6c"><span data-offset-key="a0f7a86b7abc475ab7f548a840afaa6c:0">By writing a data class instead of a plain Python class, your object instances get a few useful features out of the box that will save you some typing and manual implementation work:</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="9222a504b2d448f8ab96cec130869f56"><span data-offset-key="9222a504b2d448f8ab96cec130869f56:0">The syntax for defining instance variables is shorter, since you don’t need to implement the </span><span data-offset-key="9222a504b2d448f8ab96cec130869f56:1">`.__init__()`</span><span data-offset-key="9222a504b2d448f8ab96cec130869f56:2"> method.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="6d3546e2858944f88461def6988c2a9f"><span data-offset-key="6d3546e2858944f88461def6988c2a9f:0">Instances of your data class automatically get nice-looking string representation via an auto-generated </span><span data-offset-key="6d3546e2858944f88461def6988c2a9f:1">`.__repr__()`</span><span data-offset-key="6d3546e2858944f88461def6988c2a9f:2"> method.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="d9d0f2bec6104ee79ac0901b9728ebf2"><span data-offset-key="d9d0f2bec6104ee79ac0901b9728ebf2:0">Instance variables accept type annotations, making your data class self-documenting to a degree. Keep in mind that type annotations are just hints that are not enforced without a separate </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-type-checking/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="dc6110d91eef4ecb8c416763d82122ab"><span data-offset-key="dc6110d91eef4ecb8c416763d82122ab:0">type-checking</span></span></a><span data-key="9593b821325e4f63a9ce8debeed4eb0b"><span data-offset-key="9593b821325e4f63a9ce8debeed4eb0b:0"> tool.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="9cde5bc537da46868d145167fca4bbca"><span data-offset-key="9cde5bc537da46868d145167fca4bbca:0">Data classes are typically created using the </span><span data-offset-key="9cde5bc537da46868d145167fca4bbca:1">`@dataclass`</span><span data-offset-key="9cde5bc537da46868d145167fca4bbca:2"> </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/primer-on-python-decorators/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="8c12608d67fc485eba68b71509ed46c3"><span data-offset-key="8c12608d67fc485eba68b71509ed46c3:0">decorator</span></span></a><span data-key="70cb2de976c2421581f470c8bdbab890"><span data-offset-key="70cb2de976c2421581f470c8bdbab890:0">, as you’ll see in the code example below:</span></span></span>

     from dataclasses import dataclass​ @dataclass class Car:     color: str     mileage: float     automatic: bool​​ car1 = Car("red", 3812.4, True)​​ # Instances have a nice repr:​ car1Car(color='red', mileage=3812.4, automatic=True)​​ # Accessing fields:​ car1.mileage3812.4​​ # Fields are mutable:​ car1.mileage = 12​ car1.windshield = "broken"​​ # Type annotations are not enforced without​ # a separate type checking tool like mypy:​ Car("red", "NOT_A_FLOAT", 99)Car(color='red', mileage='NOT_A_FLOAT', automatic=99)` ​#To learn more about Python data classes, check out the [The Ultimate Guide to Data Classes in Python 3.7](https://realpython.com/python-data-classes/).

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="5544b1aa54934984b15ce5f89838138b"><span data-offset-key="5544b1aa54934984b15ce5f89838138b:0">`collections.namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="5544b1aa54934984b15ce5f89838138b:1">: Convenient Data Objects</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="569beb305c204a75b188b9a0a5cd2d4d"><span data-offset-key="569beb305c204a75b188b9a0a5cd2d4d:0">The </span></span><a href="https://dbader.org/blog/writing-clean-python-with-namedtuples" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="56de2ec81a604463bd6b8027c22741c9"><span data-offset-key="56de2ec81a604463bd6b8027c22741c9:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">namedtuple</code></span></span></a><span data-key="ea3f482aad6d42c5b9d46d6f46005a21"><span data-offset-key="ea3f482aad6d42c5b9d46d6f46005a21:0"> class available in Python 2.6+ provides an extension of the built-in </span><span data-offset-key="ea3f482aad6d42c5b9d46d6f46005a21:1">`tuple`</span><span data-offset-key="ea3f482aad6d42c5b9d46d6f46005a21:2"> data type. Similar to defining a custom class, using </span><span data-offset-key="ea3f482aad6d42c5b9d46d6f46005a21:3">`namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="ea3f482aad6d42c5b9d46d6f46005a21:4"> allows you to define reusable blueprints for your records that ensure the correct field names are used.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="624ff55d05074e9caffb43a3a695fe82"><span data-offset-key="624ff55d05074e9caffb43a3a695fe82:0">` ``pynamedtuple `</span><span data-offset-key="624ff55d05074e9caffb43a3a695fe82:1"> objects are immutable, just like regular tuples. This means you can’t add new fields or modify existing fields after the </span><span data-offset-key="624ff55d05074e9caffb43a3a695fe82:2">`namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="624ff55d05074e9caffb43a3a695fe82:3"> instance is created.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="45326505be0846bc9e883fa57359ea81"><span data-offset-key="45326505be0846bc9e883fa57359ea81:0">Besides that, </span><span data-offset-key="45326505be0846bc9e883fa57359ea81:1">`namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="45326505be0846bc9e883fa57359ea81:2"> objects are, well . . . named tuples. Each object stored in them can be accessed through a unique identifier. This frees you from having to remember integer indexes or resort to workarounds like defining </span><span data-offset-key="45326505be0846bc9e883fa57359ea81:3">**integer constants**</span><span data-offset-key="45326505be0846bc9e883fa57359ea81:4"> as mnemonics for your indexes.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="5e831c4942c540eb80ed464aa7d8b38f"><span data-offset-key="5e831c4942c540eb80ed464aa7d8b38f:0">` ``pynamedtuple `</span><span data-offset-key="5e831c4942c540eb80ed464aa7d8b38f:1"> objects are implemented as regular Python classes internally. When it comes to memory usage, they’re also better than regular classes and just as memory efficient as regular tuples:</span></span></span>

     from collections import namedtuple​ from sys import getsizeof​​ p1 = namedtuple("Point", "x y z")(1, 2, 3)​ p2 = (1, 2, 3)​​ getsizeof(p1)64​ getsizeof(p2)64`

> <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="a76da85c1a3b493d81dc971c3346b32c"><span data-offset-key="a76da85c1a3b493d81dc971c3346b32c:0">pynamedtuple\` objects can be an easy way to clean up your code and make it more readable by enforcing a better structure for your data.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="2a6d22f8bbf04da899174ad6b2166437"><span data-offset-key="2a6d22f8bbf04da899174ad6b2166437:0">I find that going from ad-hoc data types like dictionaries with a fixed format to </span><span data-offset-key="2a6d22f8bbf04da899174ad6b2166437:1">`namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="2a6d22f8bbf04da899174ad6b2166437:2"> objects helps me to express the intent of my code more clearly. Often when I apply this refactoring, I magically come up with a better solution for the problem I’m facing.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="3d34807a3afe4ff6ae63b8cf1ec77c23"><span data-offset-key="3d34807a3afe4ff6ae63b8cf1ec77c23:0">Using </span><span data-offset-key="3d34807a3afe4ff6ae63b8cf1ec77c23:1">`namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="3d34807a3afe4ff6ae63b8cf1ec77c23:2"> objects over regular (unstructured) tuples and dicts can also make your coworkers’ lives easier by making the data that’s being passed around self-documenting, at least to a degree:</span></span></span>

     from collections import namedtuple​ Car = namedtuple("Car" , "color mileage automatic")​ car1 = Car("red", 3812.4, True)​​ # Instances have a nice repr:​ car1Car(color="red", mileage=3812.4, automatic=True)​​ # Accessing fields:​ car1.mileage3812.4​​ # Fields are immtuable:​ car1.mileage = 12Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>AttributeError: can't set attribute​​ car1.windshield = "broken"Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>AttributeError: 'Car' object has no attribute 'windshield'`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="1f7900d409e9405f9251d9836cfc4dde"><span data-offset-key="1f7900d409e9405f9251d9836cfc4dde:0">`typing.NamedTuple`</span><span data-offset-key="1f7900d409e9405f9251d9836cfc4dde:1">: Improved Namedtuples</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="5e384e9514884e329995374c88494564"><span data-offset-key="5e384e9514884e329995374c88494564:0">Added in Python 3.6, </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/typing.html#typing.NamedTuple" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="dc406e2878f349b5a70641ca9101461f"><span data-offset-key="dc406e2878f349b5a70641ca9101461f:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">typing.NamedTuple</code></span></span></a><span data-key="a271a649ca134c6bb018aa9dd46f712a"><span data-offset-key="a271a649ca134c6bb018aa9dd46f712a:0"> is the younger sibling of the </span><span data-offset-key="a271a649ca134c6bb018aa9dd46f712a:1">`namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="a271a649ca134c6bb018aa9dd46f712a:2"> class in the </span><span data-offset-key="a271a649ca134c6bb018aa9dd46f712a:3">`collections`</span><span data-offset-key="a271a649ca134c6bb018aa9dd46f712a:4"> module. It’s very similar to </span><span data-offset-key="a271a649ca134c6bb018aa9dd46f712a:5">`namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="a271a649ca134c6bb018aa9dd46f712a:6">, with the main difference being an updated syntax for defining new record types and added support for </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/typing.html" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="9f286fbdd5e44ef4ac22dfe76e02fb47"><span data-offset-key="9f286fbdd5e44ef4ac22dfe76e02fb47:0">type hints</span></span></a><span data-key="37cbfacf96154b75b4b392da15a14ff1"><span data-offset-key="37cbfacf96154b75b4b392da15a14ff1:0">.</span></span></span>
