<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="21c70212118d440f94c24e65e4d007de"><span data-offset-key="21c70212118d440f94c24e65e4d007de:0">Please note that type annotations are not enforced without a separate type-checking tool like </span></span><a href="http://mypy-lang.org/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="38fb1d6b0ce24c6e9922baebc23264a3"><span data-offset-key="38fb1d6b0ce24c6e9922baebc23264a3:0">mypy</span></span></a><span data-key="e99ed95ad2f84fa6b0a4107e947c75c4"><span data-offset-key="e99ed95ad2f84fa6b0a4107e947c75c4:0">. But even without tool support, they can provide useful hints for other programmers (or be terribly confusing if the type hints become out of date):</span></span></span>

     from typing import NamedTuple​​ class Car(NamedTuple):     color: str     mileage: float     automatic: bool​​ car1 = Car("red", 3812.4, True)​​ # Instances have a nice repr:​ car1Car(color='red', mileage=3812.4, automatic=True)​​ # Accessing fields:​ car1.mileage3812.4​​ # Fields are immutable:​ car1.mileage = 12Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>AttributeError: can't set attribute​​ car1.windshield = "broken"Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>AttributeError: 'Car' object has no attribute 'windshield'​​ # Type annotations are not enforced without​ # a separate type checking tool like mypy:​ Car("red", "NOT_A_FLOAT", 99)Car(color='red', mileage='NOT_A_FLOAT', automatic=99)`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="f98d5140f1b74b688e618a198b87ccb5"><span data-offset-key="f98d5140f1b74b688e618a198b87ccb5:0">`struct.Struct`</span><span data-offset-key="f98d5140f1b74b688e618a198b87ccb5:1">: Serialized C Structs</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="abb99ba52eec47e28f8a17a862ccaef3"><span data-offset-key="abb99ba52eec47e28f8a17a862ccaef3:0">The </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/struct.html#struct.Struct" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="a0453ba57f1d4939b7b450554e6f5214"><span data-offset-key="a0453ba57f1d4939b7b450554e6f5214:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">struct.Struct</code></span></span></a><span data-key="870f795599534594b84b096f569edef6"><span data-offset-key="870f795599534594b84b096f569edef6:0"> class converts between Python values and C structs serialized into Python </span><span data-offset-key="870f795599534594b84b096f569edef6:1">`bytes`</span><span data-offset-key="870f795599534594b84b096f569edef6:2"> objects. For example, it can be used to handle binary data stored in files or coming in from network connections.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0e3d751d7a2346c19d7c5589f984b76a"><span data-offset-key="0e3d751d7a2346c19d7c5589f984b76a:0">Structs are defined using a mini language based on </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/struct.html#struct-format-strings" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="ae2844b01368440d8737b9eda298f7e2"><span data-offset-key="ae2844b01368440d8737b9eda298f7e2:0">format strings</span></span></a><span data-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e"><span data-offset-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e:0"> that allows you to define the arrangement of various C data types like </span><span data-offset-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e:1">`char`</span><span data-offset-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e:2">, </span><span data-offset-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e:3">`int`</span><span data-offset-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e:4">, and </span><span data-offset-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e:5">`long`</span><span data-offset-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e:6"> as well as their </span><span data-offset-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e:7">`unsigned`</span><span data-offset-key="0ecfd1b3d90d47d6b3a1a3c1a3f93f4e:8"> variants.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ee2b6fe39be94bd2afd356dcce3fb87b"><span data-offset-key="ee2b6fe39be94bd2afd356dcce3fb87b:0">Serialized structs are seldom used to represent data objects meant to be handled purely inside Python code. They’re intended primarily as a data exchange format rather than as a way of holding data in memory that’s only used by Python code.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="54d938c90fdf470d81e07cfedad88e11"><span data-offset-key="54d938c90fdf470d81e07cfedad88e11:0">In some cases, packing primitive data into structs may use less memory than keeping it in other data types. However, in most cases that would be quite an advanced (and probably unnecessary) optimization:</span></span></span>

     from struct import Struct​ MyStruct = Struct("i?f")​ data = MyStruct.pack(23, False, 42.0)​​ # All you get is a blob of data:​ datab'\x17\x00\x00\x00\x00\x00\x00\x00\x00\x00(B'​​ # Data blobs can be unpacked again:​ MyStruct.unpack(data)(23, False, 42.0)`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="20def55c42ab4daf9df983b45ea9ee7c"><span data-offset-key="20def55c42ab4daf9df983b45ea9ee7c:0">`types.SimpleNamespace`</span><span data-offset-key="20def55c42ab4daf9df983b45ea9ee7c:1">: Fancy Attribute Access</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="26d69019d39a48bd8339980ae8cae86d"><span data-offset-key="26d69019d39a48bd8339980ae8cae86d:0">Here’s one more slightly obscure choice for implementing data objects in Python: </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/types.html#types.SimpleNamespace" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="cf4c37ca103446dfa2a33e25219b2101"><span data-offset-key="cf4c37ca103446dfa2a33e25219b2101:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">types.SimpleNamespace</code></span></span></a><span data-key="59dc7ac13a3447ecb0d6d7ff7b4ba92c"><span data-offset-key="59dc7ac13a3447ecb0d6d7ff7b4ba92c:0">. This class was added in Python 3.3 and provides </span><span data-offset-key="59dc7ac13a3447ecb0d6d7ff7b4ba92c:1">**attribute access**</span><span data-offset-key="59dc7ac13a3447ecb0d6d7ff7b4ba92c:2"> to its namespace.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e"><span data-offset-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e:0">This means </span><span data-offset-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e:1">`SimpleNamespace`</span><span data-offset-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e:2"> instances expose all of their keys as class attributes. You can use </span><span data-offset-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e:3">`obj.key`</span><span data-offset-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e:4"> dotted attribute access instead of the </span><span data-offset-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e:5">`obj['key']`</span><span data-offset-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e:6"> square-bracket indexing syntax that’s used by regular dicts. All instances also include a meaningful </span><span data-offset-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e:7">`__repr__`</span><span data-offset-key="1cb8a8f5ff2a4dfd9463246f60f4ba4e:8"> by default.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="675f2162e2644c05948db1dc7d54fb6a"><span data-offset-key="675f2162e2644c05948db1dc7d54fb6a:0">As its name proclaims, </span><span data-offset-key="675f2162e2644c05948db1dc7d54fb6a:1">`SimpleNamespace`</span><span data-offset-key="675f2162e2644c05948db1dc7d54fb6a:2"> is simple! It’s basically a dictionary that allows attribute access and prints nicely. Attributes can be added, modified, and deleted freely:</span></span></span>

     from types import SimpleNamespace​ car1 = SimpleNamespace(color="red", mileage=3812.4, automatic=True)​​ # The default repr:​ car1namespace(automatic=True, color='red', mileage=3812.4)​​ # Instances support attribute access and are mutable:​ car1.mileage = 12​ car1.windshield = "broken"​ del car1.automatic​ car1namespace(color='red', mileage=12, windshield='broken')`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="56c8d824ee8e4ecf8474527126579f8a"><span data-offset-key="56c8d824ee8e4ecf8474527126579f8a:0">Records, Structs, and Data Objects in Python: Summary</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="2a2b733a70d44d039d93f445136a3312"><span data-offset-key="2a2b733a70d44d039d93f445136a3312:0">As you’ve seen, there’s quite a number of different options for implementing records or data objects. Which type should you use for data objects in Python? Generally your decision will depend on your use case:</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="01941dc89d5b4dc587efe9579aff8c4a"><span data-offset-key="01941dc89d5b4dc587efe9579aff8c4a:0">If you have only a few fields, then using a plain tuple object may be okay if the field order is easy to remember or field names are superfluous. For example, think of an </span><span data-offset-key="01941dc89d5b4dc587efe9579aff8c4a:1">`(x, y, z)`</span><span data-offset-key="01941dc89d5b4dc587efe9579aff8c4a:2"> point in three-dimensional space.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e5e55304464a4229a30c04ab85516ed3"><span data-offset-key="e5e55304464a4229a30c04ab85516ed3:0">If you need immutable fields, then plain tuples, </span><span data-offset-key="e5e55304464a4229a30c04ab85516ed3:1">`collections.namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="e5e55304464a4229a30c04ab85516ed3:2">, and </span><span data-offset-key="e5e55304464a4229a30c04ab85516ed3:3">`typing.NamedTuple`</span><span data-offset-key="e5e55304464a4229a30c04ab85516ed3:4"> are all good options.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="556d03cd4b3a495fab77f237590246a1"><span data-offset-key="556d03cd4b3a495fab77f237590246a1:0">If you need to lock down field names to avoid typos, then </span><span data-offset-key="556d03cd4b3a495fab77f237590246a1:1">`collections.namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="556d03cd4b3a495fab77f237590246a1:2"> and </span><span data-offset-key="556d03cd4b3a495fab77f237590246a1:3">`typing.NamedTuple`</span><span data-offset-key="556d03cd4b3a495fab77f237590246a1:4"> are your friends.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="92a583b94f48484d9b027666894d27d8"><span data-offset-key="92a583b94f48484d9b027666894d27d8:0">If you want to keep things simple, then a plain dictionary object might be a good choice due to the convenient syntax that closely resembles </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-json/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="4827367896ed4ce98bcb0288fc43e580"><span data-offset-key="4827367896ed4ce98bcb0288fc43e580:0">JSON</span></span></a><span data-key="6efa58535704496693b74e6f08da2398"><span data-offset-key="6efa58535704496693b74e6f08da2398:0">.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="fe79b891667f45a8a3e46a1d5e3271e6"><span data-offset-key="fe79b891667f45a8a3e46a1d5e3271e6:0">If you need full control over your data structure, then it’s time to write a custom class with </span><span data-offset-key="fe79b891667f45a8a3e46a1d5e3271e6:1">`@property`</span><span data-offset-key="fe79b891667f45a8a3e46a1d5e3271e6:2"> setters and getters.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="1807a66830ab4da7a72d1dbd6168bd62"><span data-offset-key="1807a66830ab4da7a72d1dbd6168bd62:0">If you need to add behavior (methods) to the object, then you should write a custom class, either from scratch, or using the </span><span data-offset-key="1807a66830ab4da7a72d1dbd6168bd62:1">`dataclass`</span><span data-offset-key="1807a66830ab4da7a72d1dbd6168bd62:2"> decorator, or by extending </span><span data-offset-key="1807a66830ab4da7a72d1dbd6168bd62:3">`collections.namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="1807a66830ab4da7a72d1dbd6168bd62:4"> or </span><span data-offset-key="1807a66830ab4da7a72d1dbd6168bd62:5">`typing.NamedTuple`</span><span data-offset-key="1807a66830ab4da7a72d1dbd6168bd62:6">.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ffa4073c8aae47679df0f741bfd048fa"><span data-offset-key="ffa4073c8aae47679df0f741bfd048fa:0">If you need to pack data tightly to serialize it to disk or to send it over the network, then it’s time to read up on </span><span data-offset-key="ffa4073c8aae47679df0f741bfd048fa:1">`struct.Struct`</span><span data-offset-key="ffa4073c8aae47679df0f741bfd048fa:2"> because this is a great use case for it!</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e737f03018044a41bc736e3ed1c1e069"><span data-offset-key="e737f03018044a41bc736e3ed1c1e069:0">If you’re looking for a safe default choice, then my general recommendation for implementing a plain record, struct, or data object in Python would be to use </span><span data-offset-key="e737f03018044a41bc736e3ed1c1e069:1">`collections.namedtuple`</span><span data-offset-key="e737f03018044a41bc736e3ed1c1e069:2"> in Python 2.x and its younger sibling, </span><span data-offset-key="e737f03018044a41bc736e3ed1c1e069:3">`typing.NamedTuple`</span><span data-offset-key="e737f03018044a41bc736e3ed1c1e069:4"> in Python 3.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH600-23f228db--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0d33a8d4e7d34e658f8be72c1ac5083b"><span data-offset-key="0d33a8d4e7d34e658f8be72c1ac5083b:0">Sets and Multisets</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="b6ab0c40eff947e78ff78d95206771aa"><span data-offset-key="b6ab0c40eff947e78ff78d95206771aa:0">In this section, you’ll see how to implement mutable and immutable </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-sets/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="03250d2611d44cc4912e41e2b550c9bb"><span data-offset-key="03250d2611d44cc4912e41e2b550c9bb:0">set</span></span></a><span data-key="4702ba1c50a94d30be17d1e605a38e77"><span data-offset-key="4702ba1c50a94d30be17d1e605a38e77:0"> and multiset (bag) data structures in Python using built-in data types and classes from the standard library.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="9a2b2b5c7ef4496c80bd15279752b83d"><span data-offset-key="9a2b2b5c7ef4496c80bd15279752b83d:0">A </span><span data-offset-key="9a2b2b5c7ef4496c80bd15279752b83d:1">**set**</span><span data-offset-key="9a2b2b5c7ef4496c80bd15279752b83d:2"> is an unordered collection of objects that doesn’t allow duplicate elements. Typically, sets are used to quickly test a value for membership in the set, to insert or delete new values from a set, and to compute the union or intersection of two sets.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="3b2b1ba1f4014bc380a60e9dbac87934"><span data-offset-key="3b2b1ba1f4014bc380a60e9dbac87934:0">In a proper set implementation, membership tests are expected to run in fast </span><span data-offset-key="3b2b1ba1f4014bc380a60e9dbac87934:1">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="3b2b1ba1f4014bc380a60e9dbac87934:2">(1) time. Union, intersection, difference, and subset operations should take </span><span data-offset-key="3b2b1ba1f4014bc380a60e9dbac87934:3">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="3b2b1ba1f4014bc380a60e9dbac87934:4">(</span><span data-offset-key="3b2b1ba1f4014bc380a60e9dbac87934:5">_n_</span><span data-offset-key="3b2b1ba1f4014bc380a60e9dbac87934:6">) time on average. The set implementations included in Python’s standard library </span></span><a href="https://wiki.python.org/moin/TimeComplexity" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="5cb68dcbf9f844d38d6d5ad6fa95001c"><span data-offset-key="5cb68dcbf9f844d38d6d5ad6fa95001c:0">follow these performance characteristics</span></span></a><span data-key="8dc7f43f553f4bbab877a9f1d1f07623"><span data-offset-key="8dc7f43f553f4bbab877a9f1d1f07623:0">.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ffc6d2d9f2dc4f878cf35c37254427f2"><span data-offset-key="ffc6d2d9f2dc4f878cf35c37254427f2:0">Just like dictionaries, sets get special treatment in Python and have some syntactic sugar that makes them easy to create. For example, the curly-brace set expression syntax and </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/list-comprehension-python/#using-set-and-dictionary-comprehensions" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="a1b08db68d5041f5b728bfb41db95e22"><span data-offset-key="a1b08db68d5041f5b728bfb41db95e22:0">set comprehensions</span></span></a><span data-key="900e14e887a3414eaa3ffca5b1236922"><span data-offset-key="900e14e887a3414eaa3ffca5b1236922:0"> allow you to conveniently define new set instances:</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e34c9c08ffec4c23ab1e0b2426d16c7f"><span data-offset-key="e34c9c08ffec4c23ab1e0b2426d16c7f:0">` ``pyvowels = {"a", "e", "i", "o", "u"} squares = {x * x for x in range(10)} `</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0dd8b55d33fc4a2ebd1b407d9444f10b"><span data-offset-key="0dd8b55d33fc4a2ebd1b407d9444f10b:0">But be careful: To create an empty set you’ll need to call the </span><span data-offset-key="0dd8b55d33fc4a2ebd1b407d9444f10b:1">`set()`</span><span data-offset-key="0dd8b55d33fc4a2ebd1b407d9444f10b:2"> constructor. Using empty curly-braces (</span><span data-offset-key="0dd8b55d33fc4a2ebd1b407d9444f10b:3">`{}`</span><span data-offset-key="0dd8b55d33fc4a2ebd1b407d9444f10b:4">) is ambiguous and will create an empty dictionary instead.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e0409ffe1ca44034a8c0da898a866220"><span data-offset-key="e0409ffe1ca44034a8c0da898a866220:0">Python and its standard library provide several set implementations. Let’s have a look at them.</span></span></span>

    ### `set`: Your Go-To Set[](https://realpython.com/python-data-structures/#set-your-go-to-set "Permanent link")​The [`set`](https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#set-types-set-frozenset) type is the built-in set implementation in Python. It’s mutable and allows for the dynamic insertion and deletion of elements.​Python’s sets are backed by the `dict` data type and share the same performance characteristics. Any [hashable](https://docs.python.org/3/glossary.html#term-hashable) object can be stored in a set:​​​​```py vowels = {"a", "e", "i", "o", "u"}​ "e" in vowelsTrue​​ letters = set("alice")​ letters.intersection(vowels){'a', 'e', 'i'}​​ vowels.add("x")​ vowels{'i', 'a', 'u', 'o', 'x', 'e'}​​ len(vowels)6`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="87736de9a31d45008f22d809282d84a5"><span data-offset-key="87736de9a31d45008f22d809282d84a5:0">`frozenset`</span><span data-offset-key="87736de9a31d45008f22d809282d84a5:1">: Immutable Sets</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="9d1501b4f8bb487fa0916367e564fb41"><span data-offset-key="9d1501b4f8bb487fa0916367e564fb41:0">The </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#set-types-set-frozenset" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="d5ccf645b3e54b29aa064793c8a05a89"><span data-offset-key="d5ccf645b3e54b29aa064793c8a05a89:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">frozenset</code></span></span></a><span data-key="8f9a1015062447eb97d8f6604144d5ad"><span data-offset-key="8f9a1015062447eb97d8f6604144d5ad:0"> class implements an immutable version of </span><span data-offset-key="8f9a1015062447eb97d8f6604144d5ad:1">`set`</span><span data-offset-key="8f9a1015062447eb97d8f6604144d5ad:2"> that can’t be changed after it’s been constructed.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="163ec78d7b63461389a0b4bbcedf1e49"><span data-offset-key="163ec78d7b63461389a0b4bbcedf1e49:0">` ``pyfrozenset `</span><span data-offset-key="163ec78d7b63461389a0b4bbcedf1e49:1"> objects are static and allow only query operations on their elements, not inserts or deletions. Because </span><span data-offset-key="163ec78d7b63461389a0b4bbcedf1e49:2">`frozenset`</span><span data-offset-key="163ec78d7b63461389a0b4bbcedf1e49:3"> objects are static and hashable, they can be used as dictionary keys or as elements of another set, something that isn’t possible with regular (mutable) </span><span data-offset-key="163ec78d7b63461389a0b4bbcedf1e49:4">`set`</span><span data-offset-key="163ec78d7b63461389a0b4bbcedf1e49:5"> objects:</span></span></span>

     vowels = frozenset({"a", "e", "i", "o", "u"})​ vowels.add("p")Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>AttributeError: 'frozenset' object has no attribute 'add'​​ # Frozensets are hashable and can​ # be used as dictionary keys:​ d = { frozenset({1, 2, 3}): "hello" }​ d[frozenset({1, 2, 3})]'hello'`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="85b9a54d315744c999ce830d5afbd819"><span data-offset-key="85b9a54d315744c999ce830d5afbd819:0">`collections.Counter`</span><span data-offset-key="85b9a54d315744c999ce830d5afbd819:1">: Multisets</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="983d737193ae4629a7d08e85ac606b5c"><span data-offset-key="983d737193ae4629a7d08e85ac606b5c:0">The </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/collections.html#collections.Counter" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="4209d7de0b614c8084ede3bef457153a"><span data-offset-key="4209d7de0b614c8084ede3bef457153a:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">collections.Counter</code></span></span></a><span data-key="11deb7391ced474198891edc53b25cfb"><span data-offset-key="11deb7391ced474198891edc53b25cfb:0"> class in the Python standard library implements a multiset, or bag, type that allows elements in the set to have more than one occurrence.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="dc1277622b064a23b9a8e831b57ee9ea"><span data-offset-key="dc1277622b064a23b9a8e831b57ee9ea:0">This is useful if you need to keep track of not only </span><span data-offset-key="dc1277622b064a23b9a8e831b57ee9ea:1">_if_</span><span data-offset-key="dc1277622b064a23b9a8e831b57ee9ea:2"> an element is part of a set, but also </span><span data-offset-key="dc1277622b064a23b9a8e831b57ee9ea:3">_how many times_</span><span data-offset-key="dc1277622b064a23b9a8e831b57ee9ea:4"> it’s included in the set:</span></span></span>

     from collections import Counter​ inventory = Counter()​​ loot = {"sword": 1, "bread": 3}​ inventory.update(loot)​ inventoryCounter({'bread': 3, 'sword': 1})​​ more_loot = {"sword": 1, "apple": 1}​ inventory.update(more_loot)​ inventoryCounter({'bread': 3, 'sword': 2, 'apple': 1})` ​One caveat for the `Counter` class is that you’ll want to be careful when counting the number of elements in a `Counter` object. Calling `len()` returns the number of _unique_ elements in the multiset, whereas the _total_ number of elements can be retrieved using `sum()`:​​​​```py len(inventory)3  # Unique elements​​ sum(inventory.values())6  # Total no. of elements`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="3c176226636049e98b6780f2372fadbb"><span data-offset-key="3c176226636049e98b6780f2372fadbb:0">Sets and Multisets in Python: Summary</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="c3d4db3911a04e478253b388e91b1e89"><span data-offset-key="c3d4db3911a04e478253b388e91b1e89:0">Sets are another useful and commonly used data structure included with Python and its standard library. Here are a few guidelines for deciding which one to use:</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="2d8a6fee8f2d4b8a8d4e55ad66342cc3"><span data-offset-key="2d8a6fee8f2d4b8a8d4e55ad66342cc3:0">If you need a mutable set, then use the built-in </span><span data-offset-key="2d8a6fee8f2d4b8a8d4e55ad66342cc3:1">`set`</span><span data-offset-key="2d8a6fee8f2d4b8a8d4e55ad66342cc3:2"> type.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="690f85ad884f451caa9d71e613979e6f"><span data-offset-key="690f85ad884f451caa9d71e613979e6f:0">If you need hashable objects that can be used as dictionary or set keys, then use a </span><span data-offset-key="690f85ad884f451caa9d71e613979e6f:1">`frozenset`</span><span data-offset-key="690f85ad884f451caa9d71e613979e6f:2">.</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="c77b1766aeac4155a27d88a8e0bab5d4"><span data-offset-key="c77b1766aeac4155a27d88a8e0bab5d4:0">If you need a multiset, or bag, data structure, then use </span><span data-offset-key="c77b1766aeac4155a27d88a8e0bab5d4:1">`collections.Counter`</span><span data-offset-key="c77b1766aeac4155a27d88a8e0bab5d4:2">.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH600-23f228db--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="a89cfbe6aaa344629fc9926fd5a975b4"><span data-offset-key="a89cfbe6aaa344629fc9926fd5a975b4:0">Stacks (LIFOs)</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa"><span data-offset-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa:0">A </span><span data-offset-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa:1">**stack**</span><span data-offset-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa:2"> is a collection of objects that supports fast </span><span data-offset-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa:3">**Last-In/First-Out**</span><span data-offset-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa:4"> (LIFO) semantics for inserts and deletes. Unlike lists or arrays, stacks typically don’t allow for random access to the objects they contain. The insert and delete operations are also often called </span><span data-offset-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa:5">**push**</span><span data-offset-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa:6"> and </span><span data-offset-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa:7">**pop**</span><span data-offset-key="de03b93b125240e4add4473c01b5bafa:8">.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ca306d1207844da58c43923462151726"><span data-offset-key="ca306d1207844da58c43923462151726:0">A useful real-world analogy for a stack data structure is a stack of plates. New plates are added to the top of the stack, and because the plates are precious and heavy, only the topmost plate can be moved. In other words, the last plate on the stack must be the first one removed (LIFO). To reach the plates that are lower down in the stack, the topmost plates must be removed one by one.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="5874ac284ef54841843afce27b48d892"><span data-offset-key="5874ac284ef54841843afce27b48d892:0">Performance-wise, a proper </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/how-to-implement-python-stack/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="dc3ba866fb2f4a7aac04dddbd39c1150"><span data-offset-key="dc3ba866fb2f4a7aac04dddbd39c1150:0">stack implementation</span></span></a><span data-key="e6b0e24bed9447de925c9bc7d0bb6777"><span data-offset-key="e6b0e24bed9447de925c9bc7d0bb6777:0"> is expected to take </span><span data-offset-key="e6b0e24bed9447de925c9bc7d0bb6777:1">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="e6b0e24bed9447de925c9bc7d0bb6777:2">(1) time for insert and delete operations.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="215298bc0b6e44ca91f4b00cdba3a102"><span data-offset-key="215298bc0b6e44ca91f4b00cdba3a102:0">Stacks have a wide range of uses in algorithms. For example, they’re used in language parsing as well as runtime memory management, which relies on a </span><span data-offset-key="215298bc0b6e44ca91f4b00cdba3a102:1">**call stack**</span><span data-offset-key="215298bc0b6e44ca91f4b00cdba3a102:2">. A short and beautiful algorithm using a stack is </span></span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Depth-first_search" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="fe85d974d5f04ed8a45ccac4cfb21eb1"><span data-offset-key="fe85d974d5f04ed8a45ccac4cfb21eb1:0">depth-first search</span></span></a><span data-key="c9ec3a07ed39413babffb5217ed10e03"><span data-offset-key="c9ec3a07ed39413babffb5217ed10e03:0"> (DFS) on a tree or graph data structure.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="8bb351f5d2ce49c6b977f2aedd6f42ee"><span data-offset-key="8bb351f5d2ce49c6b977f2aedd6f42ee:0">Python ships with several stack implementations that each have slightly different characteristics. Let’s take a look at them and compare their characteristics.</span></span></span>

    ### `list`: Simple, Built-In Stacks[](https://realpython.com/python-data-structures/#list-simple-built-in-stacks "Permanent link")​Python’s built-in `list` type [makes a decent stack data structure](https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/datastructures.html#using-lists-as-stacks) as it supports push and pop operations in [amortized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amortized_analysis) _O_(1) time.​Python’s lists are implemented as dynamic arrays internally, which means they occasionally need to resize the storage space for elements stored in them when elements are added or removed. The list over-allocates its backing storage so that not every push or pop requires resizing. As a result, you get an amortized _O_(1) time complexity for these operations.​The downside is that this makes their performance less consistent than the stable _O_(1) inserts and deletes provided by a linked list–based implementation (as you’ll see below with `collections.deque`). On the other hand, lists do provide fast _O_(1) time random access to elements on the stack, and this can be an added benefit.​There’s an important performance caveat that you should be aware of when using lists as stacks: To get the amortized _O_(1) performance for inserts and deletes, new items must be added to the _end_ of the list with the `append()` method and removed again from the end using `pop()`. For optimum performance, stacks based on Python lists should grow towards higher indexes and shrink towards lower ones.​Adding and removing from the front is much slower and takes _O_(_n_) time, as the existing elements must be shifted around to make room for the new element. This is a performance [antipattern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-pattern) that you should avoid as much as possible:​​​​```py s = []​ s.append("eat")​ s.append("sleep")​ s.append("code")​​ s['eat', 'sleep', 'code']​​ s.pop()'code'​ s.pop()'sleep'​ s.pop()'eat'​​ s.pop()Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>IndexError: pop from empty list`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="19be7b5b0f7e4fd5be4923c917d9bf41"><span data-offset-key="19be7b5b0f7e4fd5be4923c917d9bf41:0">`collections.deque`</span><span data-offset-key="19be7b5b0f7e4fd5be4923c917d9bf41:1">: Fast and Robust Stacks</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="df04f5def2a0446b98f8597c9fddedc4"><span data-offset-key="df04f5def2a0446b98f8597c9fddedc4:0">The </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/collections.html#collections.deque" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="74e712ac5db24eb6a42a253f240ae412"><span data-offset-key="74e712ac5db24eb6a42a253f240ae412:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">deque</code></span></span></a><span data-key="5a012e0004a1407baedc64a0914f3e7d"><span data-offset-key="5a012e0004a1407baedc64a0914f3e7d:0"> class implements a double-ended queue that supports adding and removing elements from either end in </span><span data-offset-key="5a012e0004a1407baedc64a0914f3e7d:1">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="5a012e0004a1407baedc64a0914f3e7d:2">(1) time (non-amortized). Because deques support adding and removing elements from either end equally well, they can serve both as queues and as stacks.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="a280313563ec4b648bd3e991ee32900b"><span data-offset-key="a280313563ec4b648bd3e991ee32900b:0">Python’s </span><span data-offset-key="a280313563ec4b648bd3e991ee32900b:1">`deque`</span><span data-offset-key="a280313563ec4b648bd3e991ee32900b:2"> objects are implemented as </span></span><a href="https://github.com/python/cpython/blob/6e8128f02e1d36e38e5866f9dc36e051caa47bc9/Modules/_collectionsmodule.c#L33-L35" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="99a767fe6ba646bfa472ec6ab2e9e7b3"><span data-offset-key="99a767fe6ba646bfa472ec6ab2e9e7b3:0">doubly-linked lists</span></span></a><span data-key="8df21eda234549779def9c53e8a24e14"><span data-offset-key="8df21eda234549779def9c53e8a24e14:0">, which gives them excellent and consistent performance for inserting and deleting elements but poor </span><span data-offset-key="8df21eda234549779def9c53e8a24e14:1">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="8df21eda234549779def9c53e8a24e14:2">(</span><span data-offset-key="8df21eda234549779def9c53e8a24e14:3">_n_</span><span data-offset-key="8df21eda234549779def9c53e8a24e14:4">) performance for randomly accessing elements in the middle of a stack.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="fc0bbd3925a54fbe90492f1c174e72a6"><span data-offset-key="fc0bbd3925a54fbe90492f1c174e72a6:0">Overall, </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/linked-lists-python/#introducing-collectionsdeque" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="b4c204c3c9c14e51acb61749ec06e5e4"><span data-offset-key="b4c204c3c9c14e51acb61749ec06e5e4:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">collections.deque</code></span><span data-offset-key="b4c204c3c9c14e51acb61749ec06e5e4:1"> is a great choice</span></span></a><span data-key="7defea76a4294e509edd5d7e7e9c0b46"><span data-offset-key="7defea76a4294e509edd5d7e7e9c0b46:0"> if you’re looking for a stack data structure in Python’s standard library that has the performance characteristics of a linked-list implementation:</span></span></span>

     from collections import deque​ s = deque()​ s.append("eat")​ s.append("sleep")​ s.append("code")​​ sdeque(['eat', 'sleep', 'code'])​​ s.pop()'code'​ s.pop()'sleep'​ s.pop()'eat'​​ s.pop()Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>IndexError: pop from an empty deque`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0e9823e65b4b46a18f28217db5fda4d8"><span data-offset-key="0e9823e65b4b46a18f28217db5fda4d8:0">`queue.LifoQueue`</span><span data-offset-key="0e9823e65b4b46a18f28217db5fda4d8:1">: Locking Semantics for Parallel Computing</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="56b5f741b4844984aab5b30c4afbd0ae"><span data-offset-key="56b5f741b4844984aab5b30c4afbd0ae:0">The </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/queue.html#queue.LifoQueue" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="dea24a13017e4b38a8868b59957bf263"><span data-offset-key="dea24a13017e4b38a8868b59957bf263:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">LifoQueue</code></span></span></a><span data-key="edb870b12d194b7c8cff8f4cbb63af7c"><span data-offset-key="edb870b12d194b7c8cff8f4cbb63af7c:0"> stack implementation in the Python standard library is synchronized and provides </span><span data-offset-key="edb870b12d194b7c8cff8f4cbb63af7c:1">**locking semantics**</span><span data-offset-key="edb870b12d194b7c8cff8f4cbb63af7c:2"> to support multiple concurrent producers and consumers.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="c933a6d54412499db7eb26d2d99c30f2"><span data-offset-key="c933a6d54412499db7eb26d2d99c30f2:0">Besides </span><span data-offset-key="c933a6d54412499db7eb26d2d99c30f2:1">`LifoQueue`</span><span data-offset-key="c933a6d54412499db7eb26d2d99c30f2:2">, the </span><span data-offset-key="c933a6d54412499db7eb26d2d99c30f2:3">`queue`</span><span data-offset-key="c933a6d54412499db7eb26d2d99c30f2:4"> module contains several other classes that implement multi-producer, multi-consumer queues that are useful for parallel computing.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="289ea9b8f1ae4433b94d8164f94a8fbc"><span data-offset-key="289ea9b8f1ae4433b94d8164f94a8fbc:0">Depending on your use case, the locking semantics might be helpful, or they might just incur unneeded overhead. In this case, you’d be better off using a </span><span data-offset-key="289ea9b8f1ae4433b94d8164f94a8fbc:1">`list`</span><span data-offset-key="289ea9b8f1ae4433b94d8164f94a8fbc:2"> or a </span><span data-offset-key="289ea9b8f1ae4433b94d8164f94a8fbc:3">`deque`</span><span data-offset-key="289ea9b8f1ae4433b94d8164f94a8fbc:4"> as a general-purpose stack:</span></span></span>

     from queue import LifoQueue​ s = LifoQueue()​ s.put("eat")​ s.put("sleep")​ s.put("code")​​ s<queue.LifoQueue object at 0x108298dd8>​​ s.get()'code'​ s.get()'sleep'​ s.get()'eat'​​ s.get_nowait()queue.Empty​​ s.get()  # Blocks/waits forever`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="b8b38755ddc64f109c96c476a1190cef"><span data-offset-key="b8b38755ddc64f109c96c476a1190cef:0">Stack Implementations in Python: Summary</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="83ceec2124db4570802959510693764b"><span data-offset-key="83ceec2124db4570802959510693764b:0">As you’ve seen, Python ships with several implementations for a stack data structure. All of them have slightly different characteristics as well as performance and usage trade-offs.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="b49508f788be41b2b47862f4b4ed9a12"><span data-offset-key="b49508f788be41b2b47862f4b4ed9a12:0">If you’re not looking for parallel processing support (or if you don’t want to handle locking and unlocking manually), then your choice comes down to the built-in </span><span data-offset-key="b49508f788be41b2b47862f4b4ed9a12:1">`list`</span><span data-offset-key="b49508f788be41b2b47862f4b4ed9a12:2"> type or </span><span data-offset-key="b49508f788be41b2b47862f4b4ed9a12:3">`collections.deque`</span><span data-offset-key="b49508f788be41b2b47862f4b4ed9a12:4">. The difference lies in the data structure used behind the scenes and overall ease of use.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="2d38352855364b1a9ccab8dde0693669"><span data-offset-key="2d38352855364b1a9ccab8dde0693669:0">**`list`**</span><span data-offset-key="2d38352855364b1a9ccab8dde0693669:1"> is backed by a dynamic array, which makes it great for fast random access but requires occasional resizing when elements are added or removed.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930"><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:0">The list over-allocates its backing storage so that not every push or pop requires resizing, and you get an amortized </span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:1">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:2">(1) time complexity for these operations. But you do need to be careful to only insert and remove items using </span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:3">`append()`</span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:4"> and </span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:5">`pop()`</span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:6">. Otherwise, performance slows down to </span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:7">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:8">(</span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:9">_n_</span><span data-offset-key="26c7cd85a8494859858fedf0249ba930:10">).</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="5f68fcebc45a4f208e5a912676968d31"><span data-offset-key="5f68fcebc45a4f208e5a912676968d31:0">**`collections.deque`**</span><span data-offset-key="5f68fcebc45a4f208e5a912676968d31:1"> is backed by a doubly-linked list, which optimizes appends and deletes at both ends and provides consistent </span><span data-offset-key="5f68fcebc45a4f208e5a912676968d31:2">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="5f68fcebc45a4f208e5a912676968d31:3">(1) performance for these operations. Not only is its performance more stable, the </span><span data-offset-key="5f68fcebc45a4f208e5a912676968d31:4">`deque`</span><span data-offset-key="5f68fcebc45a4f208e5a912676968d31:5"> class is also easier to use because you don’t have to worry about adding or removing items from the wrong end.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e0d7d48c1ac844b8bb7a51f4a4f2ad67"><span data-offset-key="e0d7d48c1ac844b8bb7a51f4a4f2ad67:0">In summary, </span><span data-offset-key="e0d7d48c1ac844b8bb7a51f4a4f2ad67:1">`collections.deque`</span><span data-offset-key="e0d7d48c1ac844b8bb7a51f4a4f2ad67:2"> is an excellent choice for implementing a stack (LIFO queue) in Python.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH600-23f228db--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="8c9e2a9979544da895ef1aa4a71ebf65"><span data-offset-key="8c9e2a9979544da895ef1aa4a71ebf65:0">Queues (FIFOs)</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="5f444beef2d84beaa48e5891d2aeb4ec"><span data-offset-key="5f444beef2d84beaa48e5891d2aeb4ec:0">In this section, you’ll see how to implement a </span><span data-offset-key="5f444beef2d84beaa48e5891d2aeb4ec:1">**First-In/First-Out**</span><span data-offset-key="5f444beef2d84beaa48e5891d2aeb4ec:2"> (FIFO) queue data structure using only built-in data types and classes from the Python standard library.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="60aacbbdc0c34300af8bfcea8f2aeea8"><span data-offset-key="60aacbbdc0c34300af8bfcea8f2aeea8:0">A </span><span data-offset-key="60aacbbdc0c34300af8bfcea8f2aeea8:1">**queue**</span><span data-offset-key="60aacbbdc0c34300af8bfcea8f2aeea8:2"> is a collection of objects that supports fast FIFO semantics for inserts and deletes. The insert and delete operations are sometimes called </span><span data-offset-key="60aacbbdc0c34300af8bfcea8f2aeea8:3">**enqueue**</span><span data-offset-key="60aacbbdc0c34300af8bfcea8f2aeea8:4"> and </span><span data-offset-key="60aacbbdc0c34300af8bfcea8f2aeea8:5">**dequeue**</span><span data-offset-key="60aacbbdc0c34300af8bfcea8f2aeea8:6">. Unlike lists or arrays, queues typically don’t allow for random access to the objects they contain.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="6ba3e3cd49744508baef4ae336bba468"><span data-offset-key="6ba3e3cd49744508baef4ae336bba468:0">Here’s a real-world analogy for a FIFO queue:</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="f10e9eff239348d49d797f9d1340d5d8"><span data-offset-key="f10e9eff239348d49d797f9d1340d5d8:0">Imagine a line of Pythonistas waiting to pick up their conference badges on day one of PyCon registration. As new people enter the conference venue and queue up to receive their badges, they join the line (enqueue) at the back of the queue. Developers receive their badges and conference swag bags and then exit the line (dequeue) at the front of the queue.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="c4d3021af93540a69fbce44e2a5d5225"><span data-offset-key="c4d3021af93540a69fbce44e2a5d5225:0">Another way to memorize the characteristics of a queue data structure is to think of it as a pipe. You add ping-pong balls to one end, and they travel to the other end, where you remove them. While the balls are in the queue (a solid metal pipe) you can’t get at them. The only way to interact with the balls in the queue is to add new ones at the back of the pipe (enqueue) or to remove them at the front (dequeue).</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b"><span data-offset-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b:0">Queues are similar to stacks. The difference between them lies in how items are removed. With a </span><span data-offset-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b:1">**queue**</span><span data-offset-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b:2">, you remove the item </span><span data-offset-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b:3">_least_</span><span data-offset-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b:4"> recently added (FIFO) but with a </span><span data-offset-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b:5">**stack**</span><span data-offset-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b:6">, you remove the item </span><span data-offset-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b:7">_most_</span><span data-offset-key="3950c998c62c42f09720278010d4bd9b:8"> recently added (LIFO).</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="a485210821404d06b4ab28eeaba26f32"><span data-offset-key="a485210821404d06b4ab28eeaba26f32:0">Performance-wise, a proper queue implementation is expected to take </span><span data-offset-key="a485210821404d06b4ab28eeaba26f32:1">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="a485210821404d06b4ab28eeaba26f32:2">(1) time for insert and delete operations. These are the two main operations performed on a queue, and in a correct implementation, they should be fast.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="18056fe98de04591bec6c69549d984d3"><span data-offset-key="18056fe98de04591bec6c69549d984d3:0">Queues have a wide range of applications in algorithms and often help solve scheduling and parallel programming problems. A short and beautiful algorithm using a queue is </span></span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Breadth-first_search" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="ee916aa47b804746954eea8ac835c390"><span data-offset-key="ee916aa47b804746954eea8ac835c390:0">breadth-first search</span></span></a><span data-key="be1349f97b004457bfc7b2b731743204"><span data-offset-key="be1349f97b004457bfc7b2b731743204:0"> (BFS) on a tree or graph data structure.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="4f725b645a3244ec8df2d318d0f8fcdf"><span data-offset-key="4f725b645a3244ec8df2d318d0f8fcdf:0">Scheduling algorithms often use </span><span data-offset-key="4f725b645a3244ec8df2d318d0f8fcdf:1">**priority queues**</span><span data-offset-key="4f725b645a3244ec8df2d318d0f8fcdf:2"> internally. These are specialized queues. Instead of retrieving the next element by insertion time, a </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-heapq-module/#data-structures-heaps-and-priority-queues" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="b865f69699024f31b1f311ba1ffeba7c"><span data-offset-key="b865f69699024f31b1f311ba1ffeba7c:0">priority queue</span></span></a><span data-key="d25c704c1bbf46c58ad30db503a37564"><span data-offset-key="d25c704c1bbf46c58ad30db503a37564:0"> retrieves the </span><span data-offset-key="d25c704c1bbf46c58ad30db503a37564:1">_highest-priority_</span><span data-offset-key="d25c704c1bbf46c58ad30db503a37564:2"> element. The priority of individual elements is decided by the queue based on the ordering applied to their keys.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="72dba323f4ad447cb4aaaa2ac886ac23"><span data-offset-key="72dba323f4ad447cb4aaaa2ac886ac23:0">A regular queue, however, won’t reorder the items it carries. Just like in the pipe example, you get out what you put in, and in exactly that order.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="1685bbd2cc1b4e03916ba26d2e30a902"><span data-offset-key="1685bbd2cc1b4e03916ba26d2e30a902:0">Python ships with several queue implementations that each have slightly different characteristics. Let’s review them.</span></span></span>

    ### `list`: Terribly Sloooow Queues[](https://realpython.com/python-data-structures/#list-terribly-sloooow-queues "Permanent link")​It’s possible to [use a regular `list` as a queue](https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/datastructures.html#using-lists-as-queues), but this is not ideal from a performance perspective. Lists are quite slow for this purpose because inserting or deleting an element at the beginning requires shifting all the other elements by one, requiring _O_(_n_) time.​Therefore, I would _not_ recommend using a `list` as a makeshift queue in Python unless you’re dealing with only a small number of elements:​​​​```py q = []​ q.append("eat")​ q.append("sleep")​ q.append("code")​​ q['eat', 'sleep', 'code']​​ # Careful: This is slow!​ q.pop(0)'eat'`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="48b0a0aaaba54a88bce0a0e1da4aec23"><span data-offset-key="48b0a0aaaba54a88bce0a0e1da4aec23:0">`collections.deque`</span><span data-offset-key="48b0a0aaaba54a88bce0a0e1da4aec23:1">: Fast and Robust Queues</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0d5edbef8e104796ac19b481b37de606"><span data-offset-key="0d5edbef8e104796ac19b481b37de606:0">The </span><span data-offset-key="0d5edbef8e104796ac19b481b37de606:1">`deque`</span><span data-offset-key="0d5edbef8e104796ac19b481b37de606:2"> class implements a double-ended queue that supports adding and removing elements from either end in </span><span data-offset-key="0d5edbef8e104796ac19b481b37de606:3">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="0d5edbef8e104796ac19b481b37de606:4">(1) time (non-amortized). Because deques support adding and removing elements from either end equally well, they can serve both as queues and as stacks.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="d46aa678e99644cc90274f9a20f172d7"><span data-offset-key="d46aa678e99644cc90274f9a20f172d7:0">Python’s </span><span data-offset-key="d46aa678e99644cc90274f9a20f172d7:1">`deque`</span><span data-offset-key="d46aa678e99644cc90274f9a20f172d7:2"> objects are implemented as doubly-linked lists. This gives them excellent and consistent performance for inserting and deleting elements, but poor </span><span data-offset-key="d46aa678e99644cc90274f9a20f172d7:3">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="d46aa678e99644cc90274f9a20f172d7:4">(</span><span data-offset-key="d46aa678e99644cc90274f9a20f172d7:5">_n_</span><span data-offset-key="d46aa678e99644cc90274f9a20f172d7:6">) performance for randomly accessing elements in the middle of the stack.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="973c71e3e42f427693caed5bf43898cd"><span data-offset-key="973c71e3e42f427693caed5bf43898cd:0">As a result, </span><span data-offset-key="973c71e3e42f427693caed5bf43898cd:1">`collections.deque`</span><span data-offset-key="973c71e3e42f427693caed5bf43898cd:2"> is a great default choice if you’re looking for a queue data structure in Python’s standard library:</span></span></span>

     from collections import deque​ q = deque()​ q.append("eat")​ q.append("sleep")​ q.append("code")​​ qdeque(['eat', 'sleep', 'code'])​​ q.popleft()'eat'​ q.popleft()'sleep'​ q.popleft()'code'​​ q.popleft()Traceback (most recent call last):  File "<stdin>", line 1, in <module>IndexError: pop from an empty deque`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="475df8e53aa041a29801b5f759c5fa24"><span data-offset-key="475df8e53aa041a29801b5f759c5fa24:0">`queue.Queue`</span><span data-offset-key="475df8e53aa041a29801b5f759c5fa24:1">: Locking Semantics for Parallel Computing</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="964b06111621473888d5dbe32224a5ad"><span data-offset-key="964b06111621473888d5dbe32224a5ad:0">The </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/queue.html#queue.Queue" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="d1261d2b234b4aa69b53c911d6820ba5"><span data-offset-key="d1261d2b234b4aa69b53c911d6820ba5:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">queue.Queue</code></span></span></a><span data-key="65b9827a848f4b62b3ec365a98987348"><span data-offset-key="65b9827a848f4b62b3ec365a98987348:0"> implementation in the Python standard library is synchronized and provides locking semantics to support multiple concurrent producers and consumers.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="e4276bd0dc4e4ad395bea48ba3e0c6d6"><span data-offset-key="e4276bd0dc4e4ad395bea48ba3e0c6d6:0">The </span><span data-offset-key="e4276bd0dc4e4ad395bea48ba3e0c6d6:1">`queue`</span><span data-offset-key="e4276bd0dc4e4ad395bea48ba3e0c6d6:2"> module contains several other classes implementing multi-producer, multi-consumer queues that are useful for parallel computing.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="62f7c8071b7c4f18bef00f4dae142bd4"><span data-offset-key="62f7c8071b7c4f18bef00f4dae142bd4:0">Depending on your use case, the locking semantics might be helpful or just incur unneeded overhead. In this case, you’d be better off using </span><span data-offset-key="62f7c8071b7c4f18bef00f4dae142bd4:1">`collections.deque`</span><span data-offset-key="62f7c8071b7c4f18bef00f4dae142bd4:2"> as a general-purpose queue:</span></span></span>

     from queue import Queue​ q = Queue()​ q.put("eat")​ q.put("sleep")​ q.put("code")​​ q<queue.Queue object at 0x1070f5b38>​​ q.get()'eat'​ q.get()'sleep'​ q.get()'code'​​ q.get_nowait()queue.Empty​​ q.get()  # Blocks/waits forever`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="737cf764dbbe4d01a9ef52167d736b9d"><span data-offset-key="737cf764dbbe4d01a9ef52167d736b9d:0">`multiprocessing.Queue`</span><span data-offset-key="737cf764dbbe4d01a9ef52167d736b9d:1">: Shared Job Queues</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="edab15c97ea7432fab2b1c9af2cac10a"><span data-offset-key="edab15c97ea7432fab2b1c9af2cac10a:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/multiprocessing.html#multiprocessing.Queue" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="cf07290ce6424ba1a9fa37f89c19ca2c"><span data-offset-key="cf07290ce6424ba1a9fa37f89c19ca2c:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">multiprocessing.Queue</code></span></span></a><span data-key="7abc99ea27044dac87e6c416b23030d2"><span data-offset-key="7abc99ea27044dac87e6c416b23030d2:0"> is a shared job queue implementation that allows queued items to be processed in parallel by multiple concurrent workers. Process-based parallelization is popular in CPython due to the </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-gil/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="df404078f82d4810a38647f23c756098"><span data-offset-key="df404078f82d4810a38647f23c756098:0">global interpreter lock</span></span></a><span data-key="340250b74e0d4326a0f290bcdb83176b"><span data-offset-key="340250b74e0d4326a0f290bcdb83176b:0"> (GIL) that prevents some forms of parallel execution on a single interpreter process.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="d7e0c991a7aa4ac78279c6707a4c4ae9"><span data-offset-key="d7e0c991a7aa4ac78279c6707a4c4ae9:0">As a specialized queue implementation meant for sharing data between processes, </span><span data-offset-key="d7e0c991a7aa4ac78279c6707a4c4ae9:1">`multiprocessing.Queue`</span><span data-offset-key="d7e0c991a7aa4ac78279c6707a4c4ae9:2"> makes it easy to distribute work across multiple processes in order to work around the GIL limitations. This type of queue can store and transfer any </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-pickle-module/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="c16bc4139622468688bc04ada4cc3107"><span data-offset-key="c16bc4139622468688bc04ada4cc3107:0">pickleable</span></span></a><span data-key="fdf5977ffe784e948ba90f2a91d8960d"><span data-offset-key="fdf5977ffe784e948ba90f2a91d8960d:0"> object across process boundaries:</span></span></span>

     from multiprocessing import Queue​ q = Queue()​ q.put("eat")​ q.put("sleep")​ q.put("code")​​ q<multiprocessing.queues.Queue object at 0x1081c12b0>​​ q.get()'eat'​ q.get()'sleep'​ q.get()'code'​​ q.get()  # Blocks/waits forever`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ebe35828e99948d58b0c264f6d461163"><span data-offset-key="ebe35828e99948d58b0c264f6d461163:0">Queues in Python: Summary</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="81ebd8a719704c27b6b09b09ac796964"><span data-offset-key="81ebd8a719704c27b6b09b09ac796964:0">Python includes several queue implementations as part of the core language and its standard library.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="45c004de72034c14a0c955ec0d19a93f"><span data-offset-key="45c004de72034c14a0c955ec0d19a93f:0">` ``pylist `</span><span data-offset-key="45c004de72034c14a0c955ec0d19a93f:1"> objects can be used as queues, but this is generally not recommended due to slow performance.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="7d1cdfb4d7384756aca8e6a196534f54"><span data-offset-key="7d1cdfb4d7384756aca8e6a196534f54:0">If you’re not looking for parallel processing support, then the implementation offered by </span><span data-offset-key="7d1cdfb4d7384756aca8e6a196534f54:1">`collections.deque`</span><span data-offset-key="7d1cdfb4d7384756aca8e6a196534f54:2"> is an excellent default choice for implementing a FIFO queue data structure in Python. It provides the performance characteristics you’d expect from a good queue implementation and can also be used as a stack (LIFO queue).</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH600-23f228db--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="016687c2b1fb4ac98a3cd230f08651c5"><span data-offset-key="016687c2b1fb4ac98a3cd230f08651c5:0">Priority Queues</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="76cf7d1cebb1424eacb4fe0221c33217"><span data-offset-key="76cf7d1cebb1424eacb4fe0221c33217:0">A </span><span data-offset-key="76cf7d1cebb1424eacb4fe0221c33217:1">**priority queue**</span><span data-offset-key="76cf7d1cebb1424eacb4fe0221c33217:2"> is a container data structure that manages a set of records with </span></span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Total_order" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="a57e28ef314b4bb9903fd943785ef579"><span data-offset-key="a57e28ef314b4bb9903fd943785ef579:0">totally-ordered</span></span></a><span data-key="54e9a12a17094ea9b0a40b07398af451"><span data-offset-key="54e9a12a17094ea9b0a40b07398af451:0"> keys to provide quick access to the record with the smallest or largest key in the set.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="4f13faa1fcfb4fdaa62870f72a5e82e1"><span data-offset-key="4f13faa1fcfb4fdaa62870f72a5e82e1:0">You can think of a priority queue as a modified queue. Instead of retrieving the next element by insertion time, it retrieves the </span><span data-offset-key="4f13faa1fcfb4fdaa62870f72a5e82e1:1">_highest-priority_</span><span data-offset-key="4f13faa1fcfb4fdaa62870f72a5e82e1:2"> element. The priority of individual elements is decided by the order applied to their keys.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="772b3c25cdfe48238e769b81af3527f2"><span data-offset-key="772b3c25cdfe48238e769b81af3527f2:0">Priority queues are commonly used for dealing with scheduling problems. For example, you might use them to give precedence to tasks with higher urgency.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="b29dbad2161e4388a9707cccdda05292"><span data-offset-key="b29dbad2161e4388a9707cccdda05292:0">Think about the job of an operating system task scheduler:</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="ee6b24d7c4114544bd1f413d4f2246bb"><span data-offset-key="ee6b24d7c4114544bd1f413d4f2246bb:0">Ideally, higher-priority tasks on the system (such as playing a real-time game) should take precedence over lower-priority tasks (such as downloading updates in the background). By organizing pending tasks in a priority queue that uses task urgency as the key, the task scheduler can quickly select the highest-priority tasks and allow them to run first.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="a2d540bff27c48e5b9955ae1429fb2f4"><span data-offset-key="a2d540bff27c48e5b9955ae1429fb2f4:0">In this section, you’ll see a few options for how you can implement priority queues in Python using built-in data structures or data structures included in Python’s standard library. Each implementation will have its own upsides and downsides, but in my mind there’s a clear winner for most common scenarios. Let’s find out which one it is.</span></span></span>

    ### `list`: Manually Sorted Queues[](https://realpython.com/python-data-structures/#list-manually-sorted-queues "Permanent link")​You can use a sorted `list` to quickly identify and delete the smallest or largest element. The downside is that inserting new elements into a list is a slow _O_(_n_) operation.​While the insertion point can be found in _O_(log _n_) time using [`bisect.insort`](https://docs.python.org/3/library/bisect.html#bisect.insort) in the standard library, this is always dominated by the slow insertion step.​Maintaining the order by appending to the list and re-sorting also takes at least _O_(_n_ log _n_) time. Another downside is that you must manually take care of re-sorting the list when new elements are inserted. It’s easy to introduce bugs by missing this step, and the burden is always on you, the developer.​This means sorted lists are only suitable as priority queues when there will be few insertions:​​​​```py q = []​ q.append((2, "code"))​ q.append((1, "eat"))​ q.append((3, "sleep"))​ # Remember to re-sort every time a new element is inserted,​ # or use bisect.insort()​ q.sort(reverse=True)​​ while q:     next_item = q.pop()     print(next_item)​(1, 'eat')(2, 'code')(3, 'sleep')`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="aa86232af0c247d6a28ee163c62ff29b"><span data-offset-key="aa86232af0c247d6a28ee163c62ff29b:0">`heapq`</span><span data-offset-key="aa86232af0c247d6a28ee163c62ff29b:1">: List-Based Binary Heaps</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0c74d4b6d1034f2c89ac6a0cb0f1837b"><span data-offset-key="0c74d4b6d1034f2c89ac6a0cb0f1837b:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/heapq.html" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="041daebf4d204c1586a6a6b6a402e907"><span data-offset-key="041daebf4d204c1586a6a6b6a402e907:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">heapq</code></span></span></a><span data-key="516fd0175bd345f0b5312c1841328f4d"><span data-offset-key="516fd0175bd345f0b5312c1841328f4d:0"> is a binary heap implementation usually backed by a plain </span><span data-offset-key="516fd0175bd345f0b5312c1841328f4d:1">`list`</span><span data-offset-key="516fd0175bd345f0b5312c1841328f4d:2">, and it supports insertion and extraction of the smallest element in </span><span data-offset-key="516fd0175bd345f0b5312c1841328f4d:3">_O_</span><span data-offset-key="516fd0175bd345f0b5312c1841328f4d:4">(log </span><span data-offset-key="516fd0175bd345f0b5312c1841328f4d:5">_n_</span><span data-offset-key="516fd0175bd345f0b5312c1841328f4d:6">) time.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="9318ce11fab54a61a1b41cc72645c8be"><span data-offset-key="9318ce11fab54a61a1b41cc72645c8be:0">This module is a good choice for </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/python-heapq-module/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="94cec602d0b54d70ae87646d45c83f72"><span data-offset-key="94cec602d0b54d70ae87646d45c83f72:0">implementing priority queues in Python</span></span></a><span data-key="f231eb733b9c4c88b2a99cd9019df321"><span data-offset-key="f231eb733b9c4c88b2a99cd9019df321:0">. Since </span><span data-offset-key="f231eb733b9c4c88b2a99cd9019df321:1">`heapq`</span><span data-offset-key="f231eb733b9c4c88b2a99cd9019df321:2"> technically provides only a min-heap implementation, </span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/heapq.html#priority-queue-implementation-notes" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="1304081068e543c98cec1a2c8820ea7c"><span data-offset-key="1304081068e543c98cec1a2c8820ea7c:0">extra steps must be taken</span></span></a><span data-key="15196f7936b940ec9ed0c2853faa953b"><span data-offset-key="15196f7936b940ec9ed0c2853faa953b:0"> to ensure sort stability and other features typically expected from a practical priority queue:</span></span></span>

     import heapq​ q = []​ heapq.heappush(q, (2, "code"))​ heapq.heappush(q, (1, "eat"))​ heapq.heappush(q, (3, "sleep"))​​ while q:     next_item = heapq.heappop(q)     print(next_item)​(1, 'eat')(2, 'code')(3, 'sleep')`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="0fa307cc6e1e4a0c846b8694efcc0cc7"><span data-offset-key="0fa307cc6e1e4a0c846b8694efcc0cc7:0">`queue.PriorityQueue`</span><span data-offset-key="0fa307cc6e1e4a0c846b8694efcc0cc7:1">: Beautiful Priority Queues</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="7d0bde5576794dc2a3826e473adf84b8"><span data-offset-key="7d0bde5576794dc2a3826e473adf84b8:0"><span data-slate-zero-width="z">​</span></span></span><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/queue.html#queue.PriorityQueue" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="94ae6de028c645c69fcedbfe21dccbae"><span data-offset-key="94ae6de028c645c69fcedbfe21dccbae:0"><code class="code-0458e21e" spellcheck="false" data-slate-leaf="true">queue.PriorityQueue</code></span></span></a><span data-key="9ef3b3d0b95d415d9a5e4c3393fe42a0"><span data-offset-key="9ef3b3d0b95d415d9a5e4c3393fe42a0:0"> uses </span><span data-offset-key="9ef3b3d0b95d415d9a5e4c3393fe42a0:1">`heapq`</span><span data-offset-key="9ef3b3d0b95d415d9a5e4c3393fe42a0:2"> internally and shares the same time and space complexities. The difference is that </span><span data-offset-key="9ef3b3d0b95d415d9a5e4c3393fe42a0:3">`PriorityQueue`</span><span data-offset-key="9ef3b3d0b95d415d9a5e4c3393fe42a0:4"> is synchronized and provides locking semantics to support multiple concurrent producers and consumers.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="5ac7ce5e955442dfa4c335afd4f335dc"><span data-offset-key="5ac7ce5e955442dfa4c335afd4f335dc:0">Depending on your use case, this might be helpful, or it might just slow your program down slightly. In any case, you might prefer the class-based interface provided by </span><span data-offset-key="5ac7ce5e955442dfa4c335afd4f335dc:1">`PriorityQueue`</span><span data-offset-key="5ac7ce5e955442dfa4c335afd4f335dc:2"> over the function-based interface provided by </span><span data-offset-key="5ac7ce5e955442dfa4c335afd4f335dc:3">`heapq`</span><span data-offset-key="5ac7ce5e955442dfa4c335afd4f335dc:4">:</span></span></span>

     from queue import PriorityQueue​ q = PriorityQueue()​ q.put((2, "code"))​ q.put((1, "eat"))​ q.put((3, "sleep"))​​ while not q.empty():     next_item = q.get()     print(next_item)​(1, 'eat')(2, 'code')(3, 'sleep')`

###

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH400-686c0942--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="3ef8d2dd98124845b052e2bf24da9358"><span data-offset-key="3ef8d2dd98124845b052e2bf24da9358:0">Priority Queues in Python: Summary</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="4defeb6b16d34c359dbf5cbe834ca1ad"><span data-offset-key="4defeb6b16d34c359dbf5cbe834ca1ad:0">Python includes several priority queue implementations ready for you to use.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="b12da4b87b864506b8fbe9c01238a5ac"><span data-offset-key="b12da4b87b864506b8fbe9c01238a5ac:0">**`queue.PriorityQueue`**</span><span data-offset-key="b12da4b87b864506b8fbe9c01238a5ac:1"> stands out from the pack with a nice object-oriented interface and a name that clearly states its intent. It should be your preferred choice.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="281701fc35274d618c9cdac79236412d"><span data-offset-key="281701fc35274d618c9cdac79236412d:0">If you’d like to avoid the locking overhead of </span><span data-offset-key="281701fc35274d618c9cdac79236412d:1">`queue.PriorityQueue`</span><span data-offset-key="281701fc35274d618c9cdac79236412d:2">, then using the </span><span data-offset-key="281701fc35274d618c9cdac79236412d:3">**`heapq`**</span><span data-offset-key="281701fc35274d618c9cdac79236412d:4"> module directly is also a good option.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--HeadingH600-23f228db--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="7047490684a74dbb8ad42ae012c186df"><span data-offset-key="7047490684a74dbb8ad42ae012c186df:0">Conclusion: Python Data Structures</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="00019c806faa4da5af7b0db8863941cb"><span data-offset-key="00019c806faa4da5af7b0db8863941cb:0">That concludes your tour of common data structures in Python. With the knowledge you’ve gained here, you’re ready to implement efficient data structures that are just right for your specific algorithm or use case.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="1a7595eba8694def9b8a3a3ec0cb7e3e"><span data-offset-key="1a7595eba8694def9b8a3a3ec0cb7e3e:0">**In this tutorial, you’ve learned:**</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="026acf8fc9f84578b7e4954bc01fdd03"><span data-offset-key="026acf8fc9f84578b7e4954bc01fdd03:0">Which common </span><span data-offset-key="026acf8fc9f84578b7e4954bc01fdd03:1">**abstract data types**</span><span data-offset-key="026acf8fc9f84578b7e4954bc01fdd03:2"> are built into the Python standard library</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="9d7908e73e9d49db8340b6a25947c1d2"><span data-offset-key="9d7908e73e9d49db8340b6a25947c1d2:0">How the most common abstract data types map to Python’s </span><span data-offset-key="9d7908e73e9d49db8340b6a25947c1d2:1">**naming scheme**</span></span></span>

- <span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="55a0c0c91a19443893f9fc0266218a2c"><span data-offset-key="55a0c0c91a19443893f9fc0266218a2c:0">How to put abstract data types to </span><span data-offset-key="55a0c0c91a19443893f9fc0266218a2c:1">**practical use**</span><span data-offset-key="55a0c0c91a19443893f9fc0266218a2c:2"> in various algorithms</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="57ecdeaef10d48289bf2585d9d3d63b1"><span data-offset-key="57ecdeaef10d48289bf2585d9d3d63b1:0">If you enjoyed what you learned in this sample from </span><span data-offset-key="57ecdeaef10d48289bf2585d9d3d63b1:1">_Python Tricks_</span><span data-offset-key="57ecdeaef10d48289bf2585d9d3d63b1:2">, then be sure to check out </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/products/python-tricks-book/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="9da3133874c64f4b9e6edf53da26805f"><span data-offset-key="9da3133874c64f4b9e6edf53da26805f:0">the rest of the book</span></span></a><span data-key="cb5dd17c0eb84431b643112c95b32ac0"><span data-offset-key="cb5dd17c0eb84431b643112c95b32ac0:0">.</span></span></span>

<span class="text-4505230f--TextH400-3033861f--textContentFamily-49a318e1"><span data-key="39433911fc674512bd374e982e8647d2"><span data-offset-key="39433911fc674512bd374e982e8647d2:0">If you’re interested in brushing up on your general data structures knowledge, then I highly recommend </span></span><a href="https://realpython.com/asins/1848000693/" class="link-a079aa82--primary-53a25e66--link-faf6c434"><span data-key="e6f09889f89a4078b6bf97508e43ae05"><span data-offset-key="e6f09889f89a4078b6bf97508e43ae05:0">Steven S. Skiena’s </span><span data-offset-key="e6f09889f89a4078b6bf97508e43ae05:1"><em>The Algorithm Design Manual</em></span></span></a><span data-key="b35efb8523b04976981a7cae0fab7578"><span data-offset-key="b35efb8523b04976981a7cae0fab7578:0">. It strikes a great balance between teaching you fundamental (and more advanced) data structures and showing you how to implement them in your code. Steve’s book was a great help in the writing of this tutorial.</span></span></span>
